Dispute on River Harim Ownership in Al-Jami' Al-Saghir
States the dispute over harim ownership between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ownership of an unpossessed dam and explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Owner of the land behind the dam owns the dam when unpossessed.

This lesson examines a classic dispute from Al-Jami' Al-Saghir regarding the harim of a river. The case involves a dam adjacent to a river with land behind it belonging to someone else, and the dam is in no one's possession. Abu Hanifa and his two companions differ on who owns the dam. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi fiqh balances property rights and the necessity of maintenance for water resources.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: the unpossessed dam belongs to the owner of the land behind it (sahib al-ard).
2
Identify the opponent's view
State the ruling of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the dam belongs to the river owner as harim for dumping mud and similar needs.
3
Understand the condition for the dispute
Note that the disagreement only arises when neither party has a sign of possession (like a planted tree or dumped mud) on the dam. If there is possession, the possessor is the owner.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa considers the land attached to the adjacent property; the river owner only has a right of use. The Sahibayn view the dam as essential for maintaining the river, thus part of its harim.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the dam belongs to the land owner, the river owner may only use it for necessary purposes without ownership. If it belongs to the river owner, the land owner has no claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجامع الصغير نهر الرجل الى جنبه مسناة ولآخر خلف المسناة ارض تلزقها وليست المسناة في يد احدهما فهي لصاحب الارض عندابي حنيفة وقال لصاحب النهر حريماً لملقى طينه وغير ذالك

وقوله وليست المسناة في يد احدهما معناه ليس لاحدهما عليه غرس ولا طين ملقى فينكشف بهذا اللفظ موضع الخلاف
And in Al-Jami' Al-Saghir: A man has a river, adjacent to it is a dam, and behind the dam is another's land adjoining it, and the dam is not in the possession of either of them. Then it belongs to the owner of the land according to Abu Hanifa, and he (Abu Hanifa) said: It belongs to the owner of the river as a harim for the dumping of his mud and other than that.

And his statement: 'and the dam is not in the possession of either of them' means: neither of them has a tree planted on it nor mud dumped, so by this wording the point of disagreement becomes clear.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents a key dispute from Al-Jami' Al-Saghir regarding the harim (precinct) of a river. The scenario: a river, a dam (masna'a) adjacent to it, and land behind the dam belonging to another person. The dam is not possessed by either the river owner or the land owner. Abu Hanifa rules that the dam's land belongs to the land owner (sahib al-ard), while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that it belongs to the river owner (sahib al-nahr) as a harim for dumping mud and other such uses. The second part clarifies that the disagreement is specifically when there is no sign of possession on the dam (no tree or mud from either party). If possession is evident, the possessor is the owner. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding harim: the land surrounding a resource is considered part of it for necessary uses, but ownership depends on who controls it.

Key Terms
حریم (harīm): The precinct or protected area around a river, well, or property that is necessary for its use and maintenance, such as for dumping mud, drawing water, or passing.
مسناة (masnāh): A dam or embankment built to hold or divert water, often adjacent to a river.
غرس (ghars): A planted tree or plant, indicating possession through cultivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute from Al-Jami' Al-Saghir, on what condition does the disagreement about the harim of the river arise?
A) When the river owner has a tree planted on the dam
B) When the land owner has mud dumped on the dam
C) When neither has any sign of possession on the dam
D) When the dam is in the possession of a third party

Key Takeaway
The harim of a river does not automatically include unpossessed adjacent land; ownership is determined by possession or attachment to the neighboring land.

Seven Legal Issues on the River Harim: Intra-Hanafi Disputes
Enumerates seven rulings on harim covering possession, planting, mud dumping, passing, and width.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven issues related to the harim of a river and identify the intra-Hanafi differences on each issue, including the practical consequence (thamarah) of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
The harim of a river carries multiple rights and ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school on planting, mud, passing, and width.

The harim (protected area) of a river is not just a strip of land; it comes with a bundle of rights and obligations. The Hanafi school has nuanced positions on who may plant trees, dump mud, or pass through this zone, and even the width itself is debated between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. This lesson unpacks seven distinct legal issues found in the text, highlighting where the school agrees and where it differs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Seven Issues
List each of the seven masail (issues) as they appear in the passage: (1) possession of a tree or mud on the dam, (2) unknown planter of a tree, (3) authority over planting, (4) dumping mud, (5) passing through the harim, (6) adoption of opinions by al-Faqih Abu Ja'far, (7) width of the harim.
The first issue is a ruling of general principle; the rest contain ikhtilaf.
2
Separate Consensus from Dispute
Determine which issues have consensus and which have ikhtilaf. For example, the first issue (possession gives entitlement) is agreed upon. The second and third (planting) have ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn. The fourth and fifth have conflicting reports. The seventh is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
Consensus issues are binding; disputed issues require tarjih.
3
Analyze the Planting Ikhtilaf
Focus on the thamarah (practical consequence) of the dispute over planting: According to Abu Hanifa, the authority (wilayah) of planting belongs to the land owner; according to the Sahibayn, it belongs to the river owner. This affects who owns the tree and who can harvest it.
This is a classic example of how different premises lead to different property claims.
4
Evaluate the Mud and Passing Issues
For dumping mud, two views are reported: some say it follows the same ikhtilaf as planting, others say the river owner always has the right unless excessive. For passing, some say Abu Hanifa prevents the river owner from passing, others say necessity allows it. Note that the text does not fully resolve these.
Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far's adoption of Abu Hanifa's view on planting and Sahibayn's view on mud shows a practical tarjih.
5
Compare the Two Opinions on Harim Width
Abu Yusuf says the harim on each side is half the river's width; Muhammad says it is the full river's width. The text notes that Muhammad's view is more convenient (arfak) for the people, which may indicate preference.
This is a direct intra-Hanafi dispute (khi>af fi al-madhhab) on the measurement of the harim.

Source Text

اما اذا كان لاحدهما عليه ذلك فصاحب الشغل اولى لانه صاحب يد ولو كان عليه غرس لا يدرى من غربه فهو من مواضع الخلاف ايضا وثمرة الاختلاف ان ولاية الغرس لصاحب الارض عنده وعندهما الصاحب النهر واما القاء الطين فقد قيل انه على الخلاف وقيل ان لصاحب النهر ذلك مالم يفحش واما المرور فقد قيل يمنع صاحب النهر عنده وقيل لا يمنع للضرورة قال الفقيه ابو جعفر اخُذُ بقوله في الغرس وبقولهما فى القاء الطين ثم عن ابى یوسف ان حريمه مقدار نصف بطن النهر من كل جانب وعن محمد مقدار بطن النهر من كل جانب وهذا ارفق بـ الناس
As for when one of them has that (i.e., a tree or mud) on it, then the one engaged (with the work) is more entitled because he is the possessor. And if there is a tree on it, not known who planted it, then it is also among the points of disagreement. The fruit of the disagreement is that the authority of planting belongs to the owner of the land according to him (Abu Hanifa), and according to them (Sahibayn) to the owner of the river. As for dumping mud, it is said that it is upon the disagreement, and it is said that the owner of the river has that right as long as it is not excessive. As for passing, it is said that the owner of the river is prevented from it according to him (Abu Hanifa), and it is said that he is not prevented due to necessity. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: I take his statement regarding planting and their statement regarding dumping mud. Then from Abu Yusuf: the harim of the river is the amount of half the belly (width) of the river on each side, and from Muhammad: the amount of the belly of the river on each side, and this is more convenient for the people.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents seven legal issues (masail) related to the harim of a river. First: if one party has a tree or mud on the dam, that party is the owner because of possession. Second: if a tree is present but its planter is unknown, it's a point of ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Third: the thamara (result) of that ikhtilaf is that the right to plant trees on the harim belongs to the land owner according to Abu Hanifa, and to the river owner according to the Sahibayn. Fourth: regarding dumping mud, there are two opinions: some say it follows the ikhtilaf, others say the river owner always has the right unless excessive. Fifth: regarding passing through the harim, some say Abu Hanifa prevents the river owner from passing, others say he is not prevented due to necessity. Sixth: Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far (a Hanafi jurist) adopts Abu Hanifa's view on planting and the Sahibayn's view on mud dumping. Seventh: the width of the harim of a river: Abu Yusuf says it is half the belly (i.e., half the width) of the river on each side, while Muhammad says it is the full width of the river on each side, and Muhammad's view is more convenient for people. These rulings reflect the nuance within the Hanafi school on the harim's extent and usage rights.

Key Terms
حريم (harīm): The protected area around a river or well that is necessary for its maintenance and use, including rights to dump mud, plant trees, and pass.
بطن النهر (baṭn al-nahr): The 'belly' or actual bed/width of the river; used as a measure for the harim.
القاء الطين (ilqā' al-ṭīn): Dumping mud removed from the river onto the harim.
ولاية الغرس (wilāyat al-ghars): The authority or right to plant trees on the harim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the view of Imam Muhammad regarding the width of the river's harim on each side?
A) Half the belly of the river
B) The full belly of the river
C) The width of the river plus an additional amount
D) There is no defined measure

Key Takeaway
The river harim is governed by multiple rights and within the Hanafi school there are differing views on planting, mud, passing, and width; al-Faqih Abu Ja'far's tarjih and the note on convenience provide guidance for the mufta bihi.

Rights on the Harim: Planting, Dumping, and Crossing
Explaining seven issues regarding the harim of a canal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on who has the right to plant trees, dump earth, and cross on the harim of a canal, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The harim is owned by the one who possesses it, but rights to plant, dump, and cross are disputed between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

This passage from al-Hidayah discusses the legal rulings concerning the harim (preserve) of a canal. The harim is the area adjacent to the canal necessary for maintenance and access. Several issues arise regarding who has the right to use this area—whether the canal-owner or the adjacent landowner. The Hanafi school has internal disagreements on these matters, which are examined here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main actants
Recognize the key parties: the landowner (sahib al-ard), the canal-owner (sahib al-nahr), and the two disciples of Abu Hanifa (al-Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
2
Understand the first issue: possession gives ownership
If someone's property (earth or tree) is on the harim, that person owns that part due to possession (qabd).
This is agreed upon.
3
Analyze the dispute on tree planting
When the planter is unknown, Imam Abu Hanifa says the landowner has the right to plant; the Sahibayn say the canal-owner does.
Faqih Abu Ja'far follows Abu Hanifa on this.
4
Examine the dispute on dumping earth
Imam Abu Hanifa denies the canal-owner the right to dump earth; the Sahibayn affirm it, but there is consensus that moderate dumping is allowed.
Faqih Abu Ja'far follows the Sahibayn on dumping.
5
Evaluate the dispute on crossing
There is a dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, the canal-owner may be prevented; but consensus holds that crossing cannot be prevented due to necessity.
Necessity overrides the dispute.
6
Review Faqih Abu Ja'far's compromise
He combines both positions: follows Abu Hanifa on planting and the Sahibayn on dumping.
This shows practical taqlid within the school.

تنبہ - ۲ حریم کی مقدار کے متعلق فتویٰ ابو یوسف کے قول پر ہے۔ مجمع الانہرص ۵۳۶ ج ۲
تنبیہ ہمارے زندگیمیں بڑی نہروں کی مالک حکومت ہے اور ان کے لئے حریم ( پٹڑی) ہوتا ہے اس کی حکومت ہی مالک ہوتی ہے جن کی زمینوں کے برابر میں کوئی نہر جارہی ہے ان کی کوئی ملکیت نہر کی پٹڑی پر نہیں ہوتی صرف ان کو اس کے اوپر کے گذرنے کا حق ہوتا ہے درخت لگانے کا حق نہیں ہوتا۔ پھر نہروں سے جو نالیاں نکالی جاتی ہیں ان پر عموما خصوصا نہر سے کچھ آگے بڑھ کر ان پر پڑی نہیں ہوتی بلکہ وہی پانی کا چھوٹا بند ہوتا ہے جس سے کوئی آدمی چل سکے جس کو ڈول کہئے۔
اس کا مالک صاحب ارض ہے البتہ اس کو یہ حق نہیں ہوتا کہ اس کو توڑ دے چونکہ ساتھ دوسرے کا حق متعلق ہے جب کبھی نالی والا اپنا حق شرب چھوڑے گا تو وہ جگہ صاحب ارض کے تصرف میں آجائے گی جیسا کہ یہ ہمارے علاقوں میں ہوتا رہتا ہے۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah volume 14. Page 55. Book of Reviving Dead Lands. If there is a tree and it is not known who planted it, then this is also a matter of dispute. And the outcome of the dispute is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the authority to plant belongs to the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn to the canal-owner. As for dumping earth, it has been said that this is also a matter of dispute, and it has been said that the canal-owner has a right to it as long as it is not excessive. As for crossing, it has been said that according to Abu Hanifa the canal-owner will be prevented, and it has been said that due to necessity he will not be prevented. Faqih Abu Ja'far said: I take Abu Hanifa's statement regarding planting trees, and the Sahibayn's statement regarding dumping earth. Then it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the harim of a canal is half the width of the inner part of the canal on each side, and from Muhammad that it is the full width of the inner part on each side. This (Muhammad's statement) is a cause of ease for the people. Commentary: In this expression, the author has mentioned seven issues. (1) If on the harim of a canal (the bank or embankment) there is earth or a tree belonging to one of them, then the one who owns this item is the owner of the harim, because the possessor has priority. The owner of the occupancy is the one for whom that land is occupied. (2) If there is a tree on the harim of the canal but its planter is unknown, then this place is also disputed. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the owner of the harim will be the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn, the canal-owner. (3) The outcome of the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the right to plant trees in the harim belongs to the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn, to the canal-owner. (4) As for the issue of whether the canal-owner has the right to dump earth on the bank, there are two opinions: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, he has no right; according to the Sahibayn, he has a right. But by consensus, he has the right to dump earth provided he does not dump so much that the landowner cannot benefit from it at all. Allama Zayla'i says: Whatever the ownership of the harim, the other party cannot be prevented from such use as does not nullify the owner's right. Therefore, the right to plant trees belongs only to the owner, but the other has the right to passage and to dump earth. (5) As for the issue of crossing, there are also two opinions on this. As before, there is dispute. But by consensus, crossing will not be prevented because it is a necessity. (6) Faqih Abu Ja'far says: I have acted on both sayings, but with some detail: I take Imam Abu Hanifa's statement regarding planting trees, and the Sahibayn's regarding dumping earth. That is, according to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, the right to plant trees is only for the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn, the canal-owner has the right to dump canal earth on it. (7) What is the extent of the harim of a canal? On this there is a difference between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the harim on each side is half the width of the canal. According to Imam Muhammad, the harim is the full width of the canal. The author of al-Hidayah said: Imam Muhammad's statement is a cause of ease for the canal owners, but the fatwa is on Imam Abu Yusuf's statement. Caution: The harim is given for dumping earth etc. to the extent of necessity, and this is the correct view as in al-Qahistani quoting from al-Tatimmah and Majma' al-Anhur. Caution 2: Regarding the extent of the harim, the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement. (Majma' al-Anhur, vol. 2, p. 536). Caution: In our times, large canals are owned by the government, and they have a harim (bank). The government is the owner of this. Those whose lands border a running canal have no ownership over the canal bank; they only have the right to cross over it. They do not have the right to plant trees. Then the channels taken out from canals, generally and especially extending forward from the canal, do not have a bank; rather, they have just a small water embankment on which a person can walk, called a 'dol'. Its owner is the landowner, but he does not have the right to break it because the right of another is attached to it. Whenever the channel-owner abandons his right to water, that place will come under the landowner's disposal, as happens in our regions.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the harim (preserve) of a canal. The author lists seven issues. The first issue: if someone's property (earth or tree) is on the harim, that person owns that part of the harim due to possession. The second issue: if a tree's planter is unknown, Imam Abu Hanifa says the landowner owns the harim, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the canal-owner owns it. The third issue applies this dispute to the right to plant trees. The fourth issue concerns dumping earth: Imam Abu Hanifa denies the right, the Sahibayn affirm it, but there is consensus that moderate dumping is allowed. The fifth issue concerns crossing: there is dispute but consensus that it cannot be prevented due to necessity. The sixth issue records Faqih Abu Ja'far's compromise: he follows Imam Abu Hanifa on tree planting and the Sahibayn on dumping earth. The seventh issue is about the measurement of the harim: Abu Yusuf says half the canal width on each side, Muhammad says full width, and the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement. The passage ends with practical notes for modern government-owned canals, clarifying that adjacent landowners have only crossing rights, not ownership or tree-planting rights.

Key Terms
حریم (harim): The preserve or protective area around a canal (or well, river) that is allocated for maintenance and access, including the bank or embankment.
صاحب ارض (sahib al-ard): The owner of the land adjacent to the canal or the land on which the harim lies.
صاحب نہر (sahib al-nahr): The owner of the canal (i.e., the one who dug it and has primary water rights).
صاحبین (al-Sahibayn): Refers to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus (ittifaq) regarding crossing over the harim?
A) The canal-owner can prevent anyone from crossing
B) Crossing is prohibited for everyone
C) Crossing cannot be prevented because of necessity
D) Only the landowner has the right to cross

Key Takeaway
The harim of a canal involves multiple rights that are disputed within the Hanafi school; the fatwa on its measurement follows Abu Yusuf, while other rights are subject to ikhtilaf.

Measurement of the Harim: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad
Explaining the measurement of the harim of a canal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two opinions on the measurement of the harim of a canal (Abu Yusuf: half width; Muhammad: full width) and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The harim of a canal is half its width on each side according to the fatwa, following Imam Abu Yusuf.

The final issue in the passage concerns the measurement of the harim – how wide the preserve should be on each side of the canal. Two companions of Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, differ on this. The author of al-Hidayah states that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view, though Muhammad's view is said to be easier for the people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Abu Yusuf: the harim on each side is half the width of the canal. Muhammad: the harim on each side is the full width of the canal.
2
Understand the reasoning
The text does not explicitly state the reasoning, but Muhammad's view is described as 'a cause of ease' because it gives more space.
3
Note the fatwa
The author states that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement, meaning the operative ruling for practice.
This is confirmed by later sources like Majma' al-Anhur.
4
Consider the modern context
Today, large canals are often government-owned, and the harim is defined by regulation. However, the classical ruling provides a baseline for private canals.

درخت ہو جس کے بارے میں یہ معلوم نہ ہو کہ اس کو کس نے لگایا ہے تو یہ بھی مواضع اختلاف میں سے ہے اور اختلاف کا ثمرہ یہ ہے کہ پودا لگانے کی ولایت امام صاحب کے یہاں زمین والے کو ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک نہر والے کو اور بہر حال مٹی ڈالنا پس کہا گیا ہے کہ یہ ( بھی ) اختلاف پر ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ نہر والے کے لئے اس کا حق ہے جب تک کہ بہت زیادہ نہ ہو اور بہر حال گذرنا پس کہا گیا ہے کہ ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک نہر والا منع کیا جائے گا اور کہا گیا ہے کہ ضرورت کی وجہ سے منع نہیں کیا جائے گا اور فقیہ ابو جعفر نے فرمایا کہ میں درخت لگانے کے سلسلہ میں ابوحنیفہ کا قول لیتا ہوں اور مٹی ڈالنے کے سلسلہ میں صاحبین کا۔ پھر ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ نہر کا حریم نہر کے اندرونی حصہ کے نصف کی مقدار ہے ہر جانب سے اور محمدؐ سے منقول ہے ہر جانب سے نہر کے اندرونی حصہ کی مقدار ہے اور یہ ( قول محمد ) لوگوں کے لئے آسانی کا باعث ہے۔
تشریح اس عبارت میں مصنف نے سات مسائل ذکر کئے ہیں،
(1) حریم نہر (پڑی یا بند ) پر دونوں میں سے کسی کی مٹی پڑی ہے یا کسی کا درخت ہے تو جس کا یہ سامان ہے وہی حریم کا مالک ہے کیونکہ قبضہ والا اولی ہوتا ہے۔ صاحب الشغل مشغولیت والا یعنی جس کے حق میں وہ زمین مشغول ہے
(۲ حریم نہر پر درخت ہے لیکن اس کے لگانے والے کا علم نہیں ہے تو یہ جگہ بھی اختلافی ہے امام صاحب کے نزدیک حریم کا مالک صاحب ارض ہو گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صاحب نہر ہوگا۔
(۳) امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے اختلاف کا ثمرہ یہ ہوگا کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک حریم میں درخت لگانے کا حق صاحب ارض کو ہوگا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صاحب نہر کو ہو گا ۔
(۴ رہا مسئلہ کہ صاحب نہر کو پٹڑی پر مٹی ڈالنے کا بھی حق ہے یا نہیں تو اس میں دو قول ہیں
ا امام صاحب کے نزدیک حق نہیں ہوگا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک حق ہوگا۔
بالا تفاق مٹی ڈالنے کا حق ہوگا بشرطیکہ اتنی مٹی نہ ڈالے کہ صاحب ارض اس سے انتفاع ہی حاصل نہ کر سکے ، علامہ زیلعی فرماتے ہیں کہ حریم کا مالک چاہے جو ہو لیکن دوسرے کو اتنے انتفاع سے منع نہیں کیا جائے گا جس سے مالک کا حق باطل نہ ہوتا ہولہذا درخت لگانے کا حق تو صرف مالک کو ہے البتہ دوسرے کو مرور اور مٹی ڈالنے کا حق ہے۔
(۵) رہا مسئلہ گذرنے کا تو اس میں بھی دو قول ہیں
۔ حسب سابق اس میں بھی اختلاف ہے۔
بالا تفاق گذرنے سے منع نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ اس میں ضرورت ہے۔
(۶ فقیہ ابو جعفر فرماتے ہیں کہ میں نے دوقولوں پر عمل کیا مگر کچھ تفصیل ہے یعنی درخت لگانے میں امام صاحب کا قول لیتا ہوں اور مٹی ڈالنے میں صاحبین کا یعنی امام صاحب کے قول کے مطابق درخت لگانے کا حق فقط صاحب ارض کو ہے اور بقول صاحبین صاحب نہر کو اس پر نہر کی مٹی ڈالنے کا حق ہے ۷) حریم شہر کی مقدار کیا ہوگی تو اس میں امام ابو سف اور امام محمد کا اختلاف ہے امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ہر جانب میں نہر کی چوڑائی کے نصف کے برابر حریم ہوگا اور امام محمد کے نزدیک نہر کی چوڑائی کے بقدر حریم ہو گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ امام محمد کا قول ارباب نہر کے لئے آسانی کا باعث ہے لیکن فتولی امام ابو یوسف کے قول پر ہے۔
تنبیہ - بقدر ضرورت مٹی وغیرہ ڈالنے کے لئے حریم ملے گا وهو الصحيح كما فى القهستاني نقلا عن التتمه مجمع الاشهرض
۲۶۳۵
تنبہ - ۲ حریم کی مقدار کے متعلق فتویٰ ابو یوسف کے قول پر ہے۔ مجمع الانہرص ۵۳۶ ج ۲
تنبیہ ہمارے زندگیمیں بڑی نہروں کی مالک حکومت ہے اور ان کے لئے حریم ( پٹڑی) ہوتا ہے اس کی حکومت ہی مالک ہوتی ہے جن کی زمینوں کے برابر میں کوئی نہر جارہی ہے ان کی کوئی ملکیت نہر کی پٹڑی پر نہیں ہوتی صرف ان کو اس کے اوپر کے گذرنے کا حق ہوتا ہے درخت لگانے کا حق نہیں ہوتا۔ پھر نہروں سے جو نالیاں نکالی جاتی ہیں ان پر عموما خصوصا نہر سے کچھ آگے بڑھ کر ان پر پڑی نہیں ہوتی بلکہ وہی پانی کا چھوٹا بند ہوتا ہے جس سے کوئی آدمی چل سکے جس کو ڈول کہئے۔
اس کا مالک صاحب ارض ہے البتہ اس کو یہ حق نہیں ہوتا کہ اس کو توڑ دے چونکہ ساتھ دوسرے کا حق متعلق ہے جب کبھی نالی والا اپنا حق شرب چھوڑے گا تو وہ جگہ صاحب ارض کے تصرف میں آجائے گی جیسا کہ یہ ہمارے علاقوں میں ہوتا رہتا ہے۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah volume 14. Page 55. Book of Reviving Dead Lands. If there is a tree and it is not known who planted it, then this is also a matter of dispute. And the outcome of the dispute is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the authority to plant belongs to the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn to the canal-owner. As for dumping earth, it has been said that this is also a matter of dispute, and it has been said that the canal-owner has a right to it as long as it is not excessive. As for crossing, it has been said that according to Abu Hanifa the canal-owner will be prevented, and it has been said that due to necessity he will not be prevented. Faqih Abu Ja'far said: I take Abu Hanifa's statement regarding planting trees, and the Sahibayn's statement regarding dumping earth. Then it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the harim of a canal is half the width of the inner part of the canal on each side, and from Muhammad that it is the full width of the inner part on each side. This (Muhammad's statement) is a cause of ease for the people. Commentary: In this expression, the author has mentioned seven issues. (1) If on the harim of a canal (the bank or embankment) there is earth or a tree belonging to one of them, then the one who owns this item is the owner of the harim, because the possessor has priority. The owner of the occupancy is the one for whom that land is occupied. (2) If there is a tree on the harim of the canal but its planter is unknown, then this place is also disputed. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the owner of the harim will be the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn, the canal-owner. (3) The outcome of the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the right to plant trees in the harim belongs to the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn, to the canal-owner. (4) As for the issue of whether the canal-owner has the right to dump earth on the bank, there are two opinions: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, he has no right; according to the Sahibayn, he has a right. But by consensus, he has the right to dump earth provided he does not dump so much that the landowner cannot benefit from it at all. Allama Zayla'i says: Whatever the ownership of the harim, the other party cannot be prevented from such use as does not nullify the owner's right. Therefore, the right to plant trees belongs only to the owner, but the other has the right to passage and to dump earth. (5) As for the issue of crossing, there are also two opinions on this. As before, there is dispute. But by consensus, crossing will not be prevented because it is a necessity. (6) Faqih Abu Ja'far says: I have acted on both sayings, but with some detail: I take Imam Abu Hanifa's statement regarding planting trees, and the Sahibayn's regarding dumping earth. That is, according to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, the right to plant trees is only for the landowner, and according to the Sahibayn, the canal-owner has the right to dump canal earth on it. (7) What is the extent of the harim of a canal? On this there is a difference between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the harim on each side is half the width of the canal. According to Imam Muhammad, the harim is the full width of the canal. The author of al-Hidayah said: Imam Muhammad's statement is a cause of ease for the canal owners, but the fatwa is on Imam Abu Yusuf's statement. Caution: The harim is given for dumping earth etc. to the extent of necessity, and this is the correct view as in al-Qahistani quoting from al-Tatimmah and Majma' al-Anhur. Caution 2: Regarding the extent of the harim, the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement. (Majma' al-Anhur, vol. 2, p. 536). Caution: In our times, large canals are owned by the government, and they have a harim (bank). The government is the owner of this. Those whose lands border a running canal have no ownership over the canal bank; they only have the right to cross over it. They do not have the right to plant trees. Then the channels taken out from canals, generally and especially extending forward from the canal, do not have a bank; rather, they have just a small water embankment on which a person can walk, called a 'dol'. Its owner is the landowner, but he does not have the right to break it because the right of another is attached to it. Whenever the channel-owner abandons his right to water, that place will come under the landowner's disposal, as happens in our regions.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the harim (preserve) of a canal. The author lists seven issues. The first issue: if someone's property (earth or tree) is on the harim, that person owns that part of the harim due to possession. The second issue: if a tree's planter is unknown, Imam Abu Hanifa says the landowner owns the harim, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the canal-owner owns it. The third issue applies this dispute to the right to plant trees. The fourth issue concerns dumping earth: Imam Abu Hanifa denies the right, the Sahibayn affirm it, but there is consensus that moderate dumping is allowed. The fifth issue concerns crossing: there is dispute but consensus that it cannot be prevented due to necessity. The sixth issue records Faqih Abu Ja'far's compromise: he follows Imam Abu Hanifa on tree planting and the Sahibayn on dumping earth. The seventh issue is about the measurement of the harim: Abu Yusuf says half the canal width on each side, Muhammad says full width, and the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement. The passage ends with practical notes for modern government-owned canals, clarifying that adjacent landowners have only crossing rights, not ownership or tree-planting rights.

Key Terms
حریم (harim): The preserve or protective area around a canal (or well, river) that is allocated for maintenance and access, including the bank or embankment.
صاحب ارض (sahib al-ard): The owner of the land adjacent to the canal or the land on which the harim lies.
صاحب نہر (sahib al-nahr): The owner of the canal (i.e., the one who dug it and has primary water rights).
صاحبین (al-Sahibayn): Refers to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi (operative) opinion on the measurement of the harim of a canal?
A) Full width of the canal on each side
B) Half the width of the canal on each side
C) No fixed measurement
D) The width of the canal bed

Key Takeaway
The harim of a canal is measured as half the canal's width on each side, based on the fatwa following Imam Abu Yusuf.

Harim of Canals: Width and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on canal harim width with practical notes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on canal harim width, identify the mufta bihi, and explain the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
Operative harim width is half the canal on each side (Abu Yusuf), though Muhammad's full-width view is easier for maintenance.

This passage addresses the protective easement (harim) alongside a canal. Two great Hanafi imams, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differed on its width. The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) follows Abu Yusuf, but understanding both positions reveals how Hanafi fiqh balances legal precision against practical ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Abu Yusuf's view (half canal width on each side) and Muhammad's view (full canal width on each side).
Both imams are within the Hanafi school.
2
Note the tarjih
Al-Hidayah comments that Muhammad's view is easier for canal owners, but the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view.
The mufta bihi is determined by later scholars, not by ease alone.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a wider harim (Muhammad) gives more space for maintenance; the narrower one (Abu Yusuf) restricts land use but is the operative ruling.
This affects adjacent landowners and canal maintenance.
4
Apply to modern context
Recognize that government-owned canals have different ownership of the harim strip; private canals follow the same harim rules but ownership may differ.
The harim is a right of use, not ownership.

كتاب احياء الموات 
نصف کے برابر حریم ہوگا اور امام محمد کا اختلاف ہے امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ہر جانب میں نہر کی چوڑائی کے نصف کے برابر حریم ہوگا اور امام محمد کے نزدیک نہر کی چوڑائی کے بقدر حریم ہو گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ امام محمد کا قول ارباب نہر کے لئے آسانی کا باعث ہے لیکن فتولی امام ابو یوسف کے قول پر ہے۔
تنبیہ - بقدر ضرورت مٹی وغیرہ ڈالنے کے لئے حریم ملے گا وهو الصحيح كما فى القهستاني نقلا عن التتمه مجمع الاشهرض
۲۶۳۵
تنبہ - ۲ حریم کی مقدار کے متعلق فتویٰ ابو یوسف کے قول پر ہے۔ مجمع الانہرص ۵۳۶ ج ۲
تنبیہ ہمارے زندگیمیں بڑی نہروں کی مالک حکومت ہے اور ان کے لئے حریم ( پٹڑی) ہوتا ہے اس کی حکومت ہی مالک ہوتی ہے جن کی زمینوں کے برابر میں کوئی نہر جارہی ہے ان کی کوئی ملکیت نہر کی پٹڑی پر نہیں ہوتی صرف ان کو اس کے اوپر کے گذرنے کا حق ہوتا ہے درخت لگانے کا حق نہیں ہوتا۔ پھر نہروں سے جو نالیاں نکالی جاتی ہیں ان پر عموما خصوصا نہر سے کچھ آگے بڑھ کر ان پر پڑی نہیں ہوتی بلکہ وہی پانی کا چھوٹا بند ہوتا ہے جس سے کوئی آدمی چل سکے جس کو ڈول کہئے۔
اس کا مالک صاحب ارض ہے البتہ اس کو یہ حق نہیں ہوتا کہ اس کو توڑ دے چونکہ ساتھ دوسرے کا حق متعلق ہے جب کبھی نالی والا اپنا حق شرب چھوڑے گا تو وہ جگہ صاحب ارض کے تصرف میں آجائے گی جیسا کہ یہ ہمارے علاقوں میں ہوتا رہتا ہے۔
Book of Reviving Dead Lands. Half of the width will be the harim, and Imam Muhammad has a difference. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, on each side the harim will be half the width of the canal, and according to Imam Muhammad, the harim will be equal to the width of the canal. The author of al-Hidayah said that Imam Muhammad's saying is easier for the canal owners, but the fatwa is on the saying of Imam Abu Yusuf. Tanbih: For depositing soil etc. as needed, the harim will be given, and that is correct as in al-Qahistani quoting from al-Tatimma, Majma' al-Ashbah. Tanbih 2: The fatwa regarding the amount of harim is on the saying of Abu Yusuf. Majma' al-Anhur. Tanbih: In our times, the government is the owner of large canals, and they have a harim (strip); the government owns it. Those whose lands are alongside a canal have no ownership over the canal strip; they only have the right to pass over it, not the right to plant trees. Then the channels that are taken out from canals generally, especially those that go forward a bit from the canal, they do not have a strip; rather they are just a small water bank on which a person can walk, called a 'dool'. Its owner is the landowner, but he does not have the right to break it because the right of another is attached to it. Whenever the channel owner gives up his right to water, that place will come into the disposal of the landowner, as happens in our areas.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage addresses the harim (protective easement) of a canal. The text first presents the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the harim extends half the canal's width on each side, while Muhammad holds it equals the full width. Al-Hidayah notes Muhammad's view is easier but the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf. The passage then clarifies that the harim is granted only for necessary purposes like depositing soil, citing al-Qahistani. A practical note explains that for government-owned canals, the government owns the harim; adjacent landowners only have a right of passage, not planting. For smaller channels (dools), the landowner owns the bed but cannot destroy it due to others' water rights. This is a practical application of harim rules.

Key Terms
حریم (harim): The protective easement area alongside a canal, used for maintenance and depositing soil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa mentioned in the passage, what is the width of the harim on each side of a canal?
A) Equal to the canal width
B) Half the canal width
C) One quarter of the canal width
D) There is no fixed width

Key Takeaway
The operative Hanafi ruling on canal harim is half the canal width on each side, based on the fatwa of Imam Abu Yusuf.

Introduction to Water Rights (Shurb) in Hanafi Fiqh
Prefatory material explaining the placement and sources of water rights chapters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the chapters on water rights are placed after reviving dead lands and identify the definition of shurb and the sources of these chapters.

Core Idea
Water rights (shurb) follow reviving dead lands because land needs water: the chapters are later additions by Khwaharzadah.

This passage introduces the chapters on water rights (masail al-shurb). It defines shurb as a share of water used by turn, explains its placement after the Book of Reviving Dead Lands, and notes that these chapters are not in the original matn but were added by later scholars. Understanding this context helps appreciate the development of Hanafi water law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of shurb
The passage defines shurb as the share of water used in turn for irrigation or livestock. Note that it is a right, not just an act.
Shurb is distinct from the act of drinking (sharb).
2
Understand the placement after reviving dead lands
The first tanbih states that water rights follow reviving dead lands because the need for water arises when reviving land.
Reviving dead lands (ihya' al-mawat) is a cause for needing water rights.
3
Recognize the source of these chapters
The second tanbih clarifies these chapters are not in Bidayat al-Mubtadi, al-Jami' al-Saghir, or Mukhtasar al-Quduri, but were added by Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzadah in his commentary.
These are later additions, not part of the original matn.
4
Distinguish between natural and man-made water sources
The third tanbih introduces the practical context: natural rivers vs. dug canals (public/private) have different rights.
This sets the stage for detailed rulings.
5
Appreciate the supplementary nature of this material
These chapters are not original to the core texts but were added by later scholars to address practical needs.
Understanding this helps trace the development of Hanafi fiqh.

فصول في مسائل الشرب
ترجمہ یہ چند فصلیں شرب کے مسائل کے بیان میں
تشریح شرب (بضمه شین ) پینا، جو شارب کا فعل ہے اور یہ شرب ہے یعنی پانی کا حصہ اور شریعت میں شرب کہتے ہیں پانی کے اس حصہ کو جس سے اپنی باری میں نفع اٹھایا جائے کھیتوں کو سیراب کرنے کے لئے یا چو پاؤں کو پلانے کے لئے اس میں مصنف تین فصلیں
بیان فرمائیں گے۔
تنبیہ ا باب اجياء الموات کے بعد مسائل شرب کو بیان کرنے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ احیاء موات میں شرب کی ضرورت پیش آتی ( کما ہو ظاہر )
تنبیہ - بداية المبتدی میں یہ تینوں فصلیں مذکور نہیں ہیں اس لئے کہ یہ فصول نہ جامع صغیر میں مذکور ہیں اور مختصر القدوری میں بلکہ ان کو شیخ الاسلام خو ہر زادہ نے اپنی شرح کتاب لشرب میں بیان کیا ہے۔ تنبیه - ۳ جن ملکوں میں جھیل یا تالاب نہیں ہوتے بلکہ نہر سے باری باری پانی دیا جاتا ہے تو ہر ایک کے لئے اس کی باری مقرر ہوتی ہے۔ پھر پیدائشی دریاؤں میں اور کھودی ہوئی عام و خاص نہروں میں فرق ہے اور ہر ایک کے ساتھ خاص و عام حقوق متعلق ہیں اس لئے یہاں اس کی تفصیل کی غرض سے پانیوں کے اقسام اور ان کے حقوق واحکام بیان کئے جارہے ہیں ۔
Chapters on Issues of Water Rights. Translation: These are several chapters concerning the issues of water rights. Explanation: Sharb (with dammah on shin) means drinking, which is the act of the drinker, and it is shurb meaning the share of water. In the Shariah, sharb refers to that share of water from which one benefits at his turn, for irrigating fields or watering animals. The author will present three chapters. Tanbih 1: The reason for stating issues of water rights after the Chapter of Reviving Dead Lands is that the need for water rights arises in reviving dead lands (as is apparent). Tanbih 2: These three chapters are not mentioned in Bidayat al-Mubtadi because they are not mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir or Mukhtasar al-Quduri; rather, Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzadah stated them in his commentary Kitab al-Shurb. Tanbih 3: In countries where there are no lakes or ponds but water is given from canals in turns, each person has a fixed turn. Then there is a difference between natural rivers and dug canals, whether public or private, and each has specific and general rights attached. Therefore, here for the sake of detail, the types of waters and their rights and rulings are being explained.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is the introductory section to the chapters on water rights (fusul fi masail al-shurb). The commentator defines shurb as the share of water used by turn for irrigation or livestock. Three tanbihs explain: (1) why these chapters follow the Book of Reviving Dead Lands – because reviving land requires water; (2) these chapters are not in the original matn (Bidayat al-Mubtadi) but were added by later commentators; (3) the practical distinction between natural rivers and man-made canals, and their respective rights. This sets the stage for the detailed water rulings that follow.

Key Terms
شرب (shurb): The right to a share of water for irrigation or drinking, used in turns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are the chapters on water rights placed after the Book of Reviving Dead Lands?
A) Because water rights are a subcategory of land revival
B) Because reviving dead lands requires water
C) Because the author forgot to include them earlier
D) Because water rights are unrelated to land revival

Key Takeaway
Water rights (shurb) are supplementary chapters introduced by later scholars to address the need for water after land revival, distinguishing between natural and man-made water sources.

The Right of Shafah: Drinking from Private Water Sources
Ruling that private water owners cannot prevent drinking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the right of shafah (drinking) from private water sources and explain its basis in shared access.

Core Idea
No one can be prevented from drinking water to quench thirst, even from a private well.

Water is essential for life, and Islamic law prioritizes access for basic needs. This passage addresses a common scenario: a person owns a well, canal, or qanat. Can they stop a thirsty traveler or an animal from drinking? The answer is clear and absolute. In this lesson, we break down the ruling and its underlying principle of communal sharing in water for drinking.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the core legal statement: the owner of a private water source (canal, well, qanat) does not have the right to prevent anyone from drinking (shafah).
The ruling is a prohibition on the owner, not an obligation on the drinker.
2
Identify the scope of the right
The right of shafah covers both humans and animals. It applies only to direct drinking (with the lips), not to taking water away in large quantities.
The term 'shafah' literally means lip, emphasizing immediate consumption.
3
Explain the basis (ta'lil)
The commentator states the reason: 'everyone shares in drinking' (ishtirak). Water is a common resource for basic needs.
This is based on the principle that water is a shared good in Islam.
4
Note the linguistic refinement
The word 'shafah' originally had a ba' and was abbreviated; its diminutive and plural forms confirm the meaning of 'lip' and by extension 'drinking'.
This linguistic note reinforces that the ruling is about drinking, not removing water.
5
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between shafah (drinking) and haul (carrying away). The right of shafah is universal; haul may be subject to owner's permission.
Hanafi fiqh limits the owner's right only for large-scale taking, not for immediate thirst.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المياه واذا كان لرجل نهر او بير او قناة فليس له ان يمنع شئيا من الشفة والشفة الشرب لبنى
آدم والبهائم
ترجمہ یہ فصل ہے پانیوں کے بیان میں اور جب کسی شخص کی نہر یا کنواں یا کاریز ہو تو اس کو یہ حق نہیں ہے کہ کسی کو ہونٹوں سے منع کرے اور شفتہ پینا ہے انسان یا چو پاؤں کا۔
تشریح کسی کی نہر یا کنویں سے اگر انسان یا جانور پانی پیئیں تو اس کو منع کرنے کا حق نہیں ہے کیونکہ پینے کے اعتبار سے اس میں سب کا
اشتراک ہے۔ تنبیہ - پانی کی فصل کو دیگر فصول پر مقدم فر مایا کیونکہ ان تمام فصول کا مقصد پانی ہے اور پانی ہی اصل ہے۔
شفة اصل شفهد تھا تخفیفاً باء کو حذف کر دیا گیا ہے، کیونکہ اس کی تصغیر شفیفہ اور اس کی جمع تکسیر شفاۃ آتی ہے اور یہ دونوں
ہر حکم کو اس کی اصل کی جانب لوٹا دیا کرتی ہیں۔ اس کے معنی ہیں ہونٹ لیکن اس سے مراد جانوروں کا اور انسانوں کا پانی پینا ہے۔
Section on Water: And if a man has a canal, a well, or an underground channel, then he does not have the right to prevent anyone from [drinking with] the lips, and al-shafah is drinking for humans and animals. Translation: This is a section on water. And when a person has a canal, well, or qanat, he does not have the right to prevent anyone from drinking with the lips; and the lip-drinking is drinking by humans or animals. Explanation: If humans or animals drink water from someone's canal or well, he does not have the right to prevent them, because in terms of drinking, everyone shares in it. Tanbih: The chapter on water is given precedence over other chapters because the purpose of all these chapters is water, and water is the basis. Shafah: Its original form was shafah, then the ba was dropped for lightness; its diminutive is shufayfah, and its broken plural is shifa'. These [formations] return every ruling to its origin. Its meaning is 'lip'; but here it refers to the drinking of water by animals and humans.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the section on water (fasl fi al-miyah). The ruling is clear: an owner of a private water source (canal, well, qanat) cannot prevent any human or animal from drinking water from it. The term 'shafah' (lip-drinking) means drinking directly with the mouth, not taking large quantities. The commentary explains that the reason is common share (ishtirak) in drinking. A tanbih notes why this water chapter is placed first: water is the fundamental element. The linguistic note on 'shafah' clarifies that it originally had a ba and was abbreviated. This right is only for quenching thirst, not for irrigation or taking water away in containers.

Key Terms
شفة (shafah): Drinking water directly with the lips, i.e., quenching thirst without taking water away.
حول (haul): Taking water away from the source (e.g., filling containers for transport), as opposed to immediate drinking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding a person who owns a well and sees an animal drinking from it?
A) He can prevent the animal to save water
B) He cannot prevent it because drinking is a common right
C) He can charge a fee for the water
D) He can prevent it if he has a prior claim

Key Takeaway
An owner of a private water source cannot prevent any human or animal from drinking directly from it, as the right of shafah is universal and absolute.

The Absolute Right to Drink and Conditional Right to Irrigate from Large Rivers
Ruling on large river water rights with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using water from large rivers for drinking and irrigation, and apply the conditions that make digging a canal permissible or prohibited.

Core Idea
Large rivers are mubah—drinking is absolute; irrigation is conditional on no public harm and no private property.

This lesson examines the second category of water under Hanafi fiqh—large rivers. We see that the right to drink (shifa) is unconditional for everyone, but the right to irrigate by digging a canal is subject to two key restrictions: no harm to the public and no encroachment on private property. The underlying rationale is the principle of preventing harm and the original permissibility of flowing water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rights
Read the passage and distinguish between the absolute right to drink (shifa) and the conditional right to irrigate (saqi) via digging a canal.
Shifa is absolute for everyone; saqi depends on conditions.
2
Analyze the conditions for irrigation
Note the two conditions: (1) the canal must not harm the general public, and (2) the canal must not be dug through someone else's property. Also note the prerequisite of reviving dead land.
The condition of harm includes floods or damage to villages.
3
Understand the legal basis
The river is originally mubah (ownerless), and the force of water prevents any claim of ownership. The obligation to prevent harm (darar) justifies the restrictions.
The maxim 'la darar wa la dirar' applies.
4
Apply the qiyas
The ruling for digging a canal for irrigation is analogized to digging for a water mill. Understand how the illa (digging a canal to utilize water) transfers the same conditions.
Qiyas jaliy: from irrigation to mill.
5
Reflect on modern implications
Consider how this classical ruling applies to modern large rivers and projects like irrigation canals or hydroelectric plants, always checking the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني ماء الأودية العظام كجيحون وسيحون ودجلة والفرات للناس فيه حق الشفة على الاطلاق وحق سقى الاراضى بان احيى واحد ارضا ميتة وكرى منه نهرا ليسقيها ان كان لايضر بالعامة ولا يكون النهر في ملك احد لانها مباحة في الاصل اذ قهر الماء يدفع قهر غيره وان كان يضر بالعامة فليس له ذلك لان دفع الضرر عنهم واجب وذلك في ان يميل الماء الى هذا الجانب اذا ضفته فيغرق القرى والاراضى وعلى هذا نصب الرحى عليه لان شق النهر للرحی کشقه للسقى به
The second (category): water of large rivers like Jayhun, Sayhun, Dajlah, and Furat. The people have the right of drinking absolutely and the right of irrigating lands when one revives dead land and digs a canal from it to irrigate it, provided it does not harm the general public and the canal is not in the ownership of anyone, because it (the river) is originally permissible (mubah), since the force of water repels the force of others. And if it harms the general public, then he does not have that (right) because removing harm from them is obligatory. That (harm) is that if its bank collapses, the water flows to that side and floods villages and lands. And upon this (same ruling) is the installation of a water mill on it, because digging a canal for a mill is like digging a canal for irrigation.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage deals with the second category of water rights under the Hanafi school: large rivers that are not privately owned. The text establishes that everyone has an absolute right to drink (shifa) from such rivers. Additionally, a person who revives dead land may dig a canal from the river to irrigate that land, but only under two conditions: first, the canal does not harm the general public (e.g., by causing flooding), and second, the canal is not dug through someone else's property. The reasoning is that these rivers are originally mubah (ownerless), and the natural force of water prevents any individual from claiming ownership through force. However, if the canal would cause harm—such as the riverbank collapsing and flooding villages—then the act becomes prohibited because preventing harm to the public is obligatory. The same ruling applies to setting up a water mill on the river, as digging for a mill is legally analogous (qiyas jaliy) to digging for irrigation. The Hanafi position is based on the principle of public benefit and prevention of harm.

Key Terms
الشفة (al-shifa): The right to drink water, as distinguished from irrigation (saqi). In this context, it is an absolute right for everyone from large rivers.
مباح (mubah): Ownerless and permissible; the water of large rivers is originally mubah, meaning it is not owned by anyone and can be used freely subject to harm conditions.
قهر (qahr): Force or dominance; the natural force of water that prevents others from claiming ownership by force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, as long as the land is revived
B) Only if he owns the land adjacent to the river
C) Provided it does not harm the general public and the canal is not on private property
D) Only after obtaining permission from the ruler

Key Takeaway
The right to drink from large rivers is absolute; the right to dig canals for irrigation or mills is conditional on preventing public harm and respecting private property.

Water in Divided Channels: Drinking Right vs Irrigation
Ruling that drinking right remains in divided water, with proof from hadith and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the right to drink from divided irrigation water and explain the proofs from hadith and necessity.

Core Idea
Drinking from divided water is a universal right; irrigation requires permission.

This lesson examines the third category of water in Hanafi fiqh: water that has been distributed into private irrigation channels. While the water is allocated for irrigation, the right to drink (shurb) remains open to all. We will explore the primary proof from the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) and the rational arguments rooted in necessity (darurah) and the concept of ihraz (possession).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Identify what the text rules: when water enters division channels (maqasim), the right to drink (shifa) is established. Irrigation (saqi) is not permitted without consent.
The ruling distinguishes between two uses: drinking (universal) and irrigation (private).
2
Examine the primary dalil: the hadith
The text cites the hadith 'People are partners in water, pasture, and fire' as the basis. This hadith originally included both drinking and irrigation, but a consensus (ijma) excluded irrigation for divided channels.
The Arabic says 'خص منه الاول وبقى الثانى' meaning the first (irrigation) was excluded, leaving the second (drinking).
3
Analyze the secondary proofs
The text provides rational arguments: a well does not confer ownership (ihraz) of the water; analogy with a deer in one's land; and necessity (darurah) because humans and animals need drinking water everywhere.
The deer analogy shows that mere presence does not equal ownership—similarly, water in a well is not owned until physically captured.
4
Understand the limitation: irrigation prohibited
The text explains that if someone wants to irrigate his own revived land, the people (right-holders) can prevent him, regardless of harm, because it is their specific right and there is no necessity.
The phrase 'لا ضرورة' indicates no urgent need comparable to drinking.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that the drinking right is absolute; irrigation from divided water is not allowed without the consent of those who hold the right to drink.
This is the standard Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث اذا دخل الماء فى المقاسم فحق الشفة ثابت والاصل فيه قوله عليه السلام الناس شركاء في ثلاث فى الماء والكلأ والنار وانه ينتظم الشرب خص منه الاول وبقى الثانى وهو الشفة ولان البير ونحوها ما وضع للاحراز ولا يملك المباح بدونه كالظبي اذا تكنس فى ارضه ولان في ابقاء الشفة ضرورة لا ۔ الانسان لا يمكنه استصحاب الماء الى كل مكان وهو محتاج اليه لنفسه وظهره فلو منع عنه افضى الى حرج عظيم. فان اراد رجل ان يسقى بذلك ارضا احياها كان لاهل النهران يمنعوه عنه اضر بهم اولم يضر لانه حق خاص لهم ولا ضرورة ولانا لو ابحنا ذلك لانقطعت منفعة الشرب
The third (category): when water enters the division channels, then the right of drinking is established. The basis for this is the saying of him (upon him peace): 'People are partners in three things: water, pasture, and fire.' And it includes drinking (shurb). The first (irrigation) was excluded from it, and the second (drinking) remains. And because a well and similar things are not placed for protection (ihraz), and the permissible thing is not owned without it, like a deer when it makes a den in one's land. And because in preserving the right of drinking there is necessity: a person cannot carry water with him everywhere, and he is in need of it for himself and his mount. So if he is prevented from it, it would lead to great hardship. If a man intends to irrigate with that (water) a land he has revived, the people of the city have the right to prevent him, whether it harms them or not, because it is a specific right for them and there is no necessity. And if we permitted that, the benefit of drinking would be cut off.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses the third category of water—water that has been channeled into division channels (maqasim) for irrigation purposes. The Hanafi ruling is clear: the right to drink (shifa) remains intact for everyone, even though the water is allocated for irrigation. The primary proof is the hadith: 'People are partners in three things: water, pasture, and fire.' This hadith originally covered both drinking (shurb) and irrigation (saqi), but the scholars excluded irrigation by consensus (ijma) in the case of divided channels, leaving only the drinking right. The text provides additional rational proofs: a well or similar source does not achieve private ownership through mere construction; it remains mubah until actual possession (ihraz) occurs. An analogy is drawn to a deer that takes shelter in one's land—it is not owned until captured. Moreover, there is a practical necessity: people and their animals need water wherever they go, and carrying water everywhere is impossible. Preventing drinking would cause extreme hardship (haraj). However, if someone wants to use such water to irrigate his own revived land, the residents of the locality can prevent him, whether it causes them harm or not. This is because the water is their specific right for drinking, and allowing irrigation would deplete the water needed for drinking. The Hanafi position balances individual rights with communal necessity.

Key Terms
المقاسم (al-maqasim): Division channels or canals that distribute water to specific lands for irrigation. After distribution, the water is under the control of the beneficiaries.
الشرب (al-shurb): The act of drinking, distinguished from saqi (irrigation). In this context, it is the only right that survives after water is divided.
الاحراز (al-ihraz): Taking physical possession or enclosure of an ownerless thing to establish ownership. Without ihraz, the thing remains mubah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary evidence for the right to drink from water that has been divided into irrigation channels?
A) The principle of necessity (darurah)
B) The Quranic verse on sharing water
C) The hadith 'People are partners in water, pasture, and fire'
D) The consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The right to drink from divided water remains a universal entitlement; irrigation is a private right that requires consent.

Two Opinions on Prohibiting Animals from a Small Stream
Two opinions on prohibiting watering at a small stream

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on prohibiting animals from a small stream and explain the analogical reasoning (qiyas) behind each.

Core Idea
A small stream owner's right to prohibit animals depends on whether the water loss is continuous like irrigation or intermittent like rotation.

Water rights are a key issue in Hanafi fiqh, particularly when a private water source is exhausted by public use. This passage from Al-Hidayah presents two contradictory opinions within the Hanafi school on whether the owner of a small stream can prevent animals from drinking when they consume all the water. The difference arises from two different analogies: one likening the situation to a daily rotation system (miyamah) where access is shared, and the other to irrigation of fields where the owner's right is absolute. You will learn to identify the underlying legal causes ('illah) and understand why both positions exist within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
The passage asks: Can the owner of a small stream (shafah) prohibit camels and livestock from drinking if they come in large numbers and exhaust the water?
The act in question is the owner's prohibition, not the animals' drinking.
2
State the first opinion and its analogy
First opinion (qīla): He cannot prohibit. The analogy (qiyas) is to miyamah (daily rotation), because animals do not come every time, so water access is intermittent like a rotation system.
Miyamah is a known method for dividing water rights, based on Quran 54:28.
3
State the second opinion and its analogy
Second opinion (qīla): He can prohibit. The analogy is to irrigating fields and orchards (saqi al-mazari'), where the owner controls all water usage to protect his right.
This analogy treats water consumption by animals as equivalent to watering crops—both exhaust the resource.
4
Identify the comprehensive reason (al-jami')
The text states that the common element (al-jāmi') is the wasting of the owner's right (tafwīt haqqihi). Both opinions accept this, but differ on whether the water loss is severe enough to trigger prohibition.
The difference hinges on the frequency and predictability of the animals' visits.
5
Note the practical implication of the ikhtilaf
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with no settled tarjih in this passage. A mufti would choose based on the specific circumstances, such as whether the animals come daily or occasionally.
Later Hanafi works may prefer one view; check for later tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشفة اذا كان ياتى على الماء كله بان كان جدولا صغيرا وفيما يرد من الابل والمواشى كثرة ينقطع الماء بشربها قيل لا يمنع منه لان الابل لا يردها فى كل وقت فصار كالمياومة وهو سبيل في قسمة الشرب وقيل اعتبارا بسقى المزارع والمشاجر والجامع تفويت حقه له ان يـ [؟]
And the small stream (shafah) when it consumes all the water, being a small channel, and among the camels and livestock that come, there is such abundance that the water is cut off by their drinking. It is said: he is not prohibited from it because the camels do not return every time, so it becomes like a daily rotation (miyamah) which is a method in dividing water rights. And it is said: by analogy to irrigating fields and orchards, and the comprehensive reason is the wasting of his right; he has the right to...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a person owns a small stream (shafah) that provides water for drinking. When large numbers of camels and livestock come to drink and consume all the water, there are two opinions on whether the owner can prevent them. The first opinion says he cannot, because the animals do not come at all times, so it is akin to a daily rotation (miyamah) system for water division. The second opinion, by analogy to irrigating crops and trees, says he can, because allowing them would waste his right. The passage is a ruling expansion from the Hidayah, with the urdu commentary clarifying that the comprehensive cause ('illah) is the loss of the owner's right.

Key Terms
الشفة (ash-shafah): A small stream or water channel, specifically a narrow watercourse that provides drinking water.
المياومة (al-miyamah): A daily rotation system for dividing water access among users, where each party gets water on alternate days or times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the comprehensive reason (al-jami') for the opinion that the owner can prohibit animals from a small stream?
A) The animals cause harm to the stream
B) The loss of the owner's right
C) The stream is too small
D) The animals drink all the time

Key Takeaway
The owner's right to prohibit animals from a small stream depends on whether the water loss is seen as intermittent (miyamah) or continuous (irrigation), both using qiyas within the Hanafi school.

Irrigating House Plants from a Stream: The Role of Custom
Ruling on carrying water in jars from a stream to irrigate house plants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking water from another's stream for personal use and explain the role of custom (urf) in deriving permissibility.

Core Idea
Custom can render permissible what would otherwise require permission.

Taking someone else's property without permission is normally prohibited, but custom plays a role in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson explores a case where carrying water in jars from a stream to irrigate house plants is allowed because people are lenient and consider prohibition base. You will learn how the soundest opinion relies on customary tolerance (urf) as a legal basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is carrying water in one's own jars from another's stream to irrigate trees or greens in one's house. The ruling in the soundest view (al-asahh) is that it is permissible (mubah).
The ruling is permissive, not obligatory.
2
Recognize the Source of Evidence
The evidence is not a text from Quran or Sunnah but customary practice (urf). The text says 'because people are lenient in this.'
Urf is a secondary source in Hanafi usul, accepted when no primary text contradicts.
3
Understand the Reasoning (Dana'ah)
Prohibiting such small-scale customary use is considered base (dana'ah) — a form of stinginess or meanness. The law avoids imposing burdens that custom deems unreasonable.
Dana'ah is a social concept; the sharī‘ah generally upholds noble character.
4
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi‘i) may require explicit permission even for small amounts, relying on the general prohibition of taking others' property without consent. The Hanafi position shows flexibility through custom.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (valid scholarly difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اراد ان يسقى شجرا او خضرا في داره حملا بجراره له ذلك فى الاصح لان الناس يتوسعون فيه ويعدون المنع من الدناءة
And if he intends to irrigate trees or greens in his house by carrying (water) in his jars, he has that right in the soundest view because people are lenient in this and consider prohibition to be base behavior.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage deals with a person who wishes to take water from a stream belonging to another, using his own jars, to irrigate trees or a garden that he has planted in his own house. The soundest (asahh) opinion holds that he may do so without explicit permission. The rationale is that people customarily tolerate such small-scale use; prohibiting it would be considered mean and base (dana'ah). Thus, by custom (urf), it is allowed. The Urdu commentary adds that the owner cannot forbid this because it is seen as a courtesy within the community.

Key Terms
الدناءة (ad-dana'ah): Baseness, meanness, or stinginess; here, it refers to the negative perception of prohibiting someone from taking a small amount of water for personal use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The owner's generosity

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) can render a normally prohibited act permissible when the amount is small and society is lenient.

Irrigating from Another's Stream: Permission Required
Ruling that explicit permission is needed to irrigate from another's stream

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on irrigating from another's stream and identify the two proofs based on termination of partnership and ownership of watercourse.

Core Idea
Irrigating from another's stream without permission is haram because it destroys the owner's water share.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a common fiqh scenario: when can you irrigate your land from a neighbor's water source? The author rules that once water is divided into shares, using another's share without permission is prohibited. Two proofs are given: the termination of partnership in water rights and the owner's absolute right over the watercourse and bank. Permission or lending (i‘arah) makes it permissible.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the act (irrigating from another's stream) and the hukm (haram without permission).
The prohibition is immediate once water enters partitioned shares (muqasim).
2
First proof: termination of partnership
When water enters the divided shares, the partnership in water rights is severed. Allowing another to use it would cut off the co-owner's share.
This is based on qiyas: the owner's share becomes private property.
3
Second proof: owner's right over watercourse
The watercourse (masil) and its bank (diffah) are the owner's property. No one may channel water through it or alter the bank without permission.
This emphasizes the owner's exclusive control over the physical infrastructure.
4
Exception: with permission
If the owner gives explicit permission (idhn) and lends (i‘arah) the water, the act becomes permissible, analogous to water in his vessel.
Lending (i‘arah) is like a loan of the water itself.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling protects ownership rights and prevents harm to the rightful owner. It is based on the principle that one cannot use another's property without consent.
This aligns with the general Hanafi rule of usufruct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس له ان يسقى من ذلك لان الماء متى دخل فى المقاسم انقطعت شركة الشرب بواحدة لان في ابقائه قطع شرب صاحبه ولان المسيل حق صاحب النهر والضفة تعلق بها حقه فلا يمكنه التسييل فيه ولا شق الضفة فان اذن له صاحبه فی ذلک و اعاره فلا باس به لانه حقه فتجرى فيه الاباحة كالماء المحرز في انائه
And he has no right to irrigate from that because when water enters into partitioned shares, the partnership in water rights is entirely cut off, since maintaining it would cut off the co-owner's share. And because the watercourse (masil) is the right of the owner of the stream, and the bank (diffah) is attached to his right; so he cannot channel water in it nor break the bank. If the owner gives him permission and lends it, then there is no harm because it is his right; permissibility flows from it, like water kept in his vessel.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage states that a person cannot irrigate his land, date-palm orchard, or any garden from someone else's stream (canal, well, or qanat) without the owner's explicit permission. Two reasons are given. First, when water is divided into shares (muqasim), the partnership in water rights (shirakat al-shurb) is terminated, because allowing others to use it would cut off the rightful owner's share. Second, the watercourse and its bank are the owner's property; no one may channel water through it or break the bank without permission. If the owner grants permission and lends the water, it becomes permissible, analogous to water contained in one's own vessel. The Urdu commentary emphasizes that taking water for drinking is allowed, but large-scale irrigation requires permission.

Key Terms
المقاسم (al-muqasim): The partitioned shares of water after division; once water enters these shares, it becomes exclusive to each owner.
المسيل (al-masil): The watercourse or channel through which water flows; it is considered part of the owner's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It cuts off the owner's share and partnership in water rights
C) The stream is too small
D) It is considered theft

Key Takeaway
Irrigating from another's stream without permission is haram because once water is divided into shares, the partnership is cut off and the owner's right over the watercourse is absolute.

Three Types of Rivers in Hanafi Law
Definition of three categories of rivers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi categories of rivers and identify whether pre-emption (shuf'ah) applies to each.

Core Idea
A river's legal status depends on ownership and division of water, determining whether pre-emption applies.

This passage from Al-Hidayah introduces an important classification in Hanafi water law. Understanding whether a river is public, common, or private governs the legal rights of irrigation, sale, and pre-emption. We will dissect each category and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Find the three types of rivers listed in the text. They are distinguished by ownership (whether owned or not) and by water division (whether divided among users or not).
The categories are: (1) not owned and water undivided, (2) owned and water divided but common, (3) owned and water divided but private.
2
Note ownership and water division for each
For each type, note whether it is owned (mamlook) and whether its water has entered into division (dakhala al-ma'u fi al-qismah).
Type 1: not owned, undivided. Type 2: owned, divided. Type 3: owned, divided.
3
Distinguish between types 2 and 3 using shuf'ah
The text says the differentiator (al-fasil) between the second and third types is the entitlement to pre-emption (shuf'ah). Type 2 (common) allows shuf'ah; type 3 (private) does not.
Shuf'ah is the right of co-owners to buy a share being sold to an outsider.
4
Apply to real examples
Match the categories to familiar rivers: large natural rivers like the Euphrates are type 1; irrigation canals shared by a group are type 2; a canal owned by one person is type 3.
The practical difference: in type 2, selling a share triggers pre-emption rights; in type 3, it does not.

Section on digging canals. Rivers are three: a river not owned by anyone and its water has not yet entered into division, like the Euphrates and similar; and an owned river whose water has entered under division, but it is common; and an owned river whose water has entered into division and it is private. And the differentiator between them is the entitlement to pre-emption (shuf'ah) and its absence. Translation: The author said that rivers are of three types. First, a river that is not owned by anyone and its water has not yet entered into divisions, like the Euphrates and its like. Second, a river that is owned, its water has entered under division, and it is common. Third, a river that is owned, its water has entered into division, and it is private. And the difference between them is the entitlement to pre-emption and its absence. Commentary: Here three types of rivers are stated: 1) Those with no owner and whose waters do not directly irrigate lands (e.g., Tigris, Euphrates, Ganges, Yamuna). 2) A river that is common but owned, and its water is divided among people. 3) Like the second, but with the difference that the second is common and this is private. Then what is the difference between common and private? In this, the author has various statements...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (fasl) on digging canals (kari al-anhar) and defines three legal categories of rivers. Type 1: Public rivers not owned by anyone, not yet divided for irrigation (e.g., large natural rivers). Type 2: Privately owned common rivers (am) — water is divided among multiple shareholders. Type 3: Privately owned private rivers (khas) — water belongs to one owner. The practical distinction between type 2 and 3 is the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah): in a common river, shareholders have a right of pre-emption when shares are sold; in a private river, there is no such right because there are no co-owners.

Key Terms
نهر (nahr): A river or water channel. Here legally classified into three types.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption to buy a share in common property.
مملوک (mamlook): Privately owned (said of rivers).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The three categories of rivers are distinguished by ownership and water division, with shuf'ah applying only to common rivers.
