Digging and Repair of a Common Canal: Obligation and Compulsion
Explains digging obligation and compulsion for second type canal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on digging a common canal, identify when compulsion is applied, and explain the distinction between certain and hypothetical harm.

Core Idea
Digging a common canal is wajib on its owners; refusal leads to compulsion to prevent general harm.

This passage from al-Hidayah explains the legal ruling for a second type of canal – a shared waterway owned by a group but serving a general benefit. The author clarifies that the digging cost falls on the owners, not the public treasury. If an owner refuses to participate, he is compelled by the ruler, because his refusal causes definite harm to the other owners. The text also distinguishes between certain harm (digging necessity) and hypothetical harm (future breach), affecting whether compulsion is justified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of canal
The passage divides canals into two types; this lesson covers the second type, which is owned by a group but serves a general irrigation purpose.
This is not a public canal funded by the treasury, but a shared private canal.
2
Determine who bears the digging cost
The text explicitly states 'كريه على اهله' (digging is upon its owners). The treasury is not responsible.
Benefit of the canal returns specifically to the owners, so they bear the cost.
3
Understand the compulsion rule
If an owner refuses to contribute to digging, the ruler compels him. The justification is removal of general harm (ضرر عام) to the other partners.
The refuser's individual hardship (ضرر خاص) is outweighed because general harm takes precedence.
4
Distinguish certain vs. hypothetical harm in repairs
For fortification to prevent breach, compulsion only applies if the danger is real (e.g., imminent flooding). If the risk is hypothetical (موهوم), no compulsion.
Digging is considered a certain necessity, while repairs may be uncertain.
5
Apply the principle of preferring general harm removal
The overarching legal principle is that removing general harm is prioritized over specific harm, even if the specific harm involves labor and expense.
This is a key maxim in Hanafi fiqh applied to shared utilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second type: As for the second, its digging is upon its owners, not upon the treasury, because the right is theirs and the benefit returns to them specifically and exclusively. Whoever among them refuses is compelled to dig it to remove general harm, which is the harm to the remaining partners. The harm to the refuser is specific and is met with compensation, so it does not oppose [the general harm]. If they intend to fortify it out of fear of breach, and there is general harm such as flooding of lands and spoiling of roads, the refuser is compelled; otherwise not, because it is hypothetical, unlike digging because it is certain. Translation: And the second type of canal, its digging is upon its owners, not upon the treasury, because the right is for them and the benefit returns to them specifically and exclusively. Whoever among them refuses is compelled to dig it to remove general harm, which is the harm to the remaining partners. The harm to the refuser is specific and is met with compensation, so specific harm does not oppose general harm. If they intend to fortify it out of fear of breach, and there is general harm such as flooding of lands and spoiling of roads, the refuser is compelled; otherwise not, because it is hypothetical, unlike digging because it is certain. Commentary: The second type of canal, which is owned by its owners but is general, will be dug by those who have a right in it. Its digging is not the duty of the ruler, because it is their specific and exclusive right. Hence, if one of them refuses to dig, he is compelled to remove general harm, i.e., the harm to the remaining partners. Here an objection may arise: he also suffers harm because he has to labor and spend money. He said: this harm is specific, and his benefit will be greater as he will irrigate his fields. Therefore, to remove general harm, specific harm is tolerated. The author said: 'so it does not oppose' meaning there is no conflict between them; rather, general harm prevails, so striving to remove general harm is obligatory. If there is a danger that the canal's bank may break causing great damage, and the people want to strengthen it so it does not break, the refuser is compelled. If there is no such danger, then there is no compulsion for a hypothetical matter. Question: Then why was compulsion applied for digging? Answer: Its benefit and necessity are not hypothetical but certain.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the second type of canal, which is owned by a group but serves a general purpose (e.g., irrigating multiple lands). The text states that the cost of digging is on the owners, not the public treasury, because the benefit is specific to them. If an owner refuses to contribute, he is compelled to do so because his refusal causes general harm to the other owners. The refuser's individual harm (labor and expense) is specific and is outweighed by the general harm. The passage then discusses repair: if there is a genuine risk of breach causing widespread damage (like flooding), refusers are compelled; if the risk is hypothetical, no compulsion. The commentary elaborates that the ruling is based on the principle of removing general harm over specific harm. It also clarifies that digging is considered a certain necessity, while repair may be hypothetical unless the danger is real.

Key Terms
ضرر عام (darar 'amm): Harm that affects many people, such as flooding or spoiling roads.
ضرر خاص (darar khass): Harm specific to an individual, such as labor and expense.
جبر (jabr): Legal compulsion by the ruler to force an individual to act, here to dig or repair a canal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The public treasury (Bayt al-Mal)
B) The owners of the canal
C) The ruler alone
D) All residents of the area

Key Takeaway
Digging a shared canal is wajib on its owners; refusal leads to compulsion to remove certain general harm, while hypothetical risks do not justify compulsion.

Compulsion for Digging a Fully Private Canal: Two Hanafi Opinions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on excusing a canal co-owner from forced contribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on compelling a refuser to dig a fully private canal and explain the legal basis for each opinion.

Core Idea
When harm is specific to co-owners, compulsion is replaced by financial recourse.

In this passage, we encounter a subtle intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about a canal that is fully private — owned and used only by a specific group. While the obligation to dig is clear, the question is whether a refusing co-owner can be forced to participate. The text presents two views, each grounded in a different understanding of harm and remedy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of canal
The third type is 'khass min kulli wajh' — fully private, no public right. Understand its legal context.
Contrast with the second type where the canal serves a public interest.
2
State the obligation to dig
Both opinions agree that digging is wajib (obligatory) upon the owners; the dispute is about compulsion (jabr).
3
Analyze the first opinion: compulsion
Some Hanafi scholars analogize this case to the second type (which involves general harm) and rule that the refuser can be compelled to dig or contribute.
The illa (effective cause) is the existence of harm, though here the harm is specific.
4
Analyze the second opinion: no compulsion
Other scholars argue that since both harms (the refuser's potential loss and the other owners' loss) are specific, compulsion is not justified. Instead, the remedy is financial recourse: the others dig and recover the refuser's share by court order.
The recovery (ruju') is only valid if the spending was ordered by a judge.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If compulsion is not applied, the other owners must first dig and then claim costs. If compulsion is applied, the refuser can be forced to dig or pay upfront.
The text does not give a clear tarjih, leaving both opinions as valid within the school.

Source Text

تیسری قسم

ترجمہ: اور بہر حال تیسری قسم اور وہ ہر طرح سے خاص ہے تو اس کا کھودنا اس کے اہل کے ذمہ ہے اسی دلیل کی وجہ سے جو کہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں پھر کہا گیا ہے کہ انکار کرنے والے کو مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ جیسے دوسری میں اور کہا گیا ہے کہ مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ دونوں ضرروں میں سے ہر ایک خاص ہے اور ممکن ہے اس کا دور کرنا ان سے انکار کرنے والے پر رجوع کرنے کے ساتھ اس مال کے سلسلہ میں جو انھوں نے اس میں خرچ کیا ہے جبکہ خرچ کرنا قاضی کے حکم سے ہو تو دونوں جانب برابر ہو گئیں بخلاف اس صورت کے جو مقدم ہوتی ہے۔
تشریح: تیسری قسم کی نہر بھی وہی لوگ کھو دیں گے جو اس سے نفع اٹھائیں گے اسی دلیل سے جو نہر ثانی کے بیان میں گذر چکی ہے ۔ پھر اگر کوئی انکار کرے تو اس پر جبر کیا جائے گا یا نہیں تو اس میں دو قول ہیں (۱) جبر ہو گا، کیوں ، جیسے ثانی میں جبر کیا گیا تھا (۲) جبر نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ دونوں ضرر خاص ہیں لہذ ا سابق دلیل یہاں جاری نہ ہوگی نیز ضرر کا دفعیہ اس طرح ہو سکتا ہے کہ یہ لوگ کھودیں اور اس کے حصہ میں جوصرفہ، بیٹھتا ہو وہ اس سے وصول کر لیں مگر یہ وصول کرنا جب درست ہوگا جبکہ انہوں نے جو خرچ کیا ہے وہ خرچ قاضی کے حکم سے ہو تو دونوں جانبین یہاں برابر ہیں ضرر کے خاص ہونے میں بھی اور خرچ لے کر دونوں ایک درجہ میں ہونے کے اعتبار سے بھی رہا مسئلہ قسم ثانی کا تو وہاں ضرر خاص کے مقابلہ میں ضرر عام تھا اس لئے وہاں جبر کیا گیا تھا۔
Third type: As for the third, which is private from every aspect, its digging is upon its owners for the reason we mentioned. Then it is said that the refuser is compelled as in the second type, and it is said that he is not compelled because each of the two harms is specific, and it is possible to remove it from them by recourse against the refuser for what they spent, if it was by the order of the judge, so the two sides become equal, unlike what preceded. Translation: And the third type is private in every way, so its digging is upon its owners for the same reason we have stated. Then it has been said that the refuser will be compelled as in the second type, and it has been said that he will not be compelled because both harms are specific, and it is possible to remove the harm from them by recourse against the refuser for the wealth they spent, provided the spending was by the judge's order, so both sides are equal, unlike the previous case. Commentary: The third type of canal will also be dug by those who benefit from it, for the same reason mentioned in the second type. Then if someone refuses, will he be compelled? There are two sayings: (1) He will be compelled, as in the second type. (2) He will not be compelled because both harms are specific, so the previous reasoning does not apply here. Also, the harm can be removed by them digging and then recovering the cost from the refuser's share, but this recovery is only valid if the spending was by the judge's order. Thus the two sides are equal, both in terms of specific harm and in terms of recovering cost, so they are at the same level. As for the second type, there general harm was compared to specific harm, so compulsion was applied there.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage discusses the third type of canal, which is fully private (e.g., a canal owned by one person or a group but only they use it). The text first states that digging is upon the owners, as with the second type. Then it presents two opinions on compulsion: some say the refuser is compelled like the second type; others say not, because both harms (the refuser's and the others') are specific, and the others can dig and then recover their costs from the refuser by court order, making both sides equal. The commentary explains that the second type had general harm (affecting many), so compulsion was justified; here since harm is specific only to the owners, compulsion is not necessary.

Key Terms
خاص من كل وجه (khass min kulli wajh): A canal that is fully private; no public right of use.
رجوع (ruju'): Legal recourse to recover expenses from a co-owner who refuses to contribute.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion: forcing a person to perform an act against their will.

No Compulsion for the Right of Drinking
States ruling on compulsion for haqq al-shafa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on compulsion for haqq al-shafa and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this ruling.

Core Idea
The right to drink from a private canal does not give the public the power to force owners to dig it.

This lesson examines a specific right known as haqq al-shafa—the right of drinking water from a canal. The central question is whether this right can justify compelling the owners of a private canal to dig or maintain it. The Hanafi school's relied-upon view is that no compulsion is allowed, even if many people depend on the water. However, some later Hanafi scholars argued for compulsion. Understanding this ruling clarifies the limits of property rights versus communal needs in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific act is being ruled upon? Here it is compelling partners to dig a private canal for the sake of those who have a right to drink. The ruling is 'la jabr'—no compulsion is allowed.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The passage considers an objection: if many drinkers will be harmed, is that not general harm requiring compulsion? It responds that the owners' specific harm cannot be overridden by the drinkers' general harm because the canal is private.
The principle is that a specific harm (to owners) is not outweighed by a general harm (to drinkers) in this context.
3
Distinguish the authority levels
The text declares that no compulsion is the zahir al-madhhab (apparent position of the school). It then notes that some later scholars (mutakhirun) held that the ruler can compel.
Zahir al-madhhab is from the core texts; later opinions are of lesser authority unless accepted by the majority.
4
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a private canal owned by two partners. Villagers have a customary right to drink from it. The partners refuse to dig a new branch. Can the ruler force them? According to the relied-upon view, no.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) is the zahir al-madhhab: no compulsion. However, a mufti in a later period might consider the mutakhirun view in certain circumstances, but the text emphasizes the apparent position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جبـــــــر لــــحـــــق الشفة اذا امتـعـواجـ
ترجمہ: اور پینے کے حق کی وجہ سے جبر نہ ہوگا جیسا کہ جب یہ سب لوگ رک جائیں۔
تشریح: ایک نہر خاص ہے آپنے فرمایا کہ انکار کرنے والے پر جبر نہ ہو گا لیکن یہاں بھی ضرر عام کا تقابل ضرر خاص سے ہے لہذا یہاں بھی جبر واجب ہونا چاہے سوال کیسے؟ اس لئے کہ جو لوگ اس سے پانی پیئیں گے عام ہیں تو اگر نہر نہ کھودی تو انکا ضرر ہوگا تو فرمایا کہ ان لوگوں کے حق کی وجہ سے جبر نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ اگر تمام شرکا ، کھودنے سے باز رہیں تو پینے والوں کی وجہ سے ان پر جبر نہیں ہوسکتا اور یہی ظاہر مذہب ہے اور بعض متاخرین نے کہا ہے کہ حق شفعہ کی وجہ سے امام لوگوں کو نہر کھودنے پر مجبور کرے گا کذافی الکفایہ۔
And no compulsion due to the right of drinking when they all desist. Translation: And there will be no compulsion due to the right of drinking, as when all these people stop. Commentary: It is a private canal. You said that the refuser will not be compelled, but here also general harm is compared to specific harm, so compulsion should be obligatory. Why? Because those who will drink water from it are general, so if the canal is not dug, they will be harmed. He said: Due to the right of these people, there will be no compulsion, because if all the partners refrain from digging, they cannot be compelled due to the drinkers. This is the apparent (zahir) madhhab. Some later scholars said that due to the right of shafa (pre-emption), the ruler will compelpeople to dig the canal. As in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage addresses an exception: even if there are people who have a right to drink from a private canal (i.e., the right of shafa or thirst), this does not give grounds to compel the owners to dig it. The commentary raises a possible objection: if the drinkers are many, their harm is general, so compulsion should apply. But the text states that if all partners refuse to dig, the drinkers' right does not force them. This is the relied-upon (zahir al-madhhab) view. Some later scholars (mutakhirun) hold that the ruler can compel for the sake of the right of shafa, as mentioned in Al-Kifayah.

Key Terms
حق الشفة (haqq al-shafa): Right of drinking water from a canal; a right of thirst.
ظاہر مذہب (zahir al-madhhab): The apparent and relied-upon position of the school in contrast to opinions of later scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The ruler must compel owners to dig
B) There is no compulsion
C) Only if the canal is public
D) Compulsion is always required

Key Takeaway
The right to drink from a private canal does not give the ruler or the public the power to force owners to dig or maintain it; this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Cost Distribution for a Shared Canal: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cost allocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on cost allocation for digging a shared canal and explain the ratio legis (illa) behind each position.

Core Idea
The cost of digging a shared canal falls on upstream owners alone (Abu Hanifa) or proportionally on all (Sahibayn) based on benefit from downstream drainage.

When landowners share a canal, who pays for digging it? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn), differed on this question. This lesson unpacks their reasoning, focusing on whether the upstream owner benefits from the downstream section. The practical consequence changes who bears the financial burden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What is the legal question? The passage asks: On whom does the expense of digging a shared canal fall?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa says the cost is borne only by owners whose lands are upstream; once digging passes a person's land, that person is no longer liable.
Implicit illa: immediate benefit from the dug section.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the cost is shared by all owners from start to end, proportional to their shares of water (shurb) and land area.
4
Analyse the illa behind Sahibayn's view
The Sahibayn argue that the upstream owner needs the lower part for drainage of surplus water, so he benefits from the entire canal and must share the total cost.
This is the talil (ratio legis) that drives the difference.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical result: Under Abu Hanifa, upstream owners pay the entire cost; under Sahibayn, each pays in proportion to water share, making the downstream owners contribute even though digging starts upstream.
The passage does not give a tarjih (preference) between the two views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومؤنة كرى النهر المشترك عليهم من اعلاه فاذا جاوز ارض رجل رفع عنه وهذا عند ابي حنيفة وقالاهي عليهم جميعاً من اوله الى اخره بحصص الشرب الارضين لان لصاحب الاعلى حقاً في الاسفل لاحتياجه الى تسييل ما فضل من الماء فيه
ترجمہ: اور مشترک نہر کھودنے کا صرفہ ان کے اوپر نہر کے بالائی حصہ سے ہوگا پس جب کھو دائی کسی شخص کی زمین سے آگے بڑھ گئی تو اس سے صرفہ اٹھا لیا جائے گا۔ اور یہ ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ صرفہ ان تمام پر ہوگا اس کے اول سے آخر تک شرب اور زمینوں کے حصوں کے بقدر اس لئے کہ صاحب اعلیٰ کے لئے حق ہے اسفل میں اس کے محتاج ہونے کی وجہ سے اسفل میں اس پانی کے بہانے کی جانب جو بچ گیا ہے۔
تشریح: (Urdu commentary not provided in OCR? Actually the OCR ends there. But with context from the surrounding text, we can infer.)
And the expense of digging a shared canal is upon them from its top; when it passes the land of a man, it is lifted from him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions (Sahibayn) said: It is upon all of them from its beginning to its end according to the shares of drink and lands, because the owner of the upper part has a right in the lower part due to his need for the flow of surplus water. Translation: And the cost of digging a shared canal will be upon them from the upper part. So when the digging passes beyond a man's land, the cost is removed from him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions said that the cost will be upon all of them from beginning to end according to the shares of drink and lands, because the owner of the upper part has a right in the lower part due to his need for the flow of surplus water.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on how to distribute the cost of digging a shared canal. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the cost is borne only by those whose lands are upstream; once the digging passes a person's land, they are no longer liable. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn), the cost is shared by all owners from start to end, proportional to their share of water and land area. Their reasoning is that upstream owners need the downstream section to carry away surplus water, so they benefit from the entire canal. The textual operation tag is expansion_probe because the commentary expands on the matn.

Key Terms
مؤنة (mu'na): Cost, expense, or expenditure.
شرب (shurb): Share of water (right of irrigation).
صاحب اعلی (sahib al-a'la): Owner of the upstream portion of the canal.

Key Takeaway

Compulsion to Dig a Canal: The Third Type and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
States ruling on third-type canal and two opinions on compulsion of refusers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi opinions on compulsion of refusers in digging a specific-benefit canal and evaluate the rationale behind each opinion.

Core Idea
When harm is specific on both sides, financial recourse replaces compulsion — the second view prevails.

This passage addresses a practical dispute in irrigation law: if a canal benefits only a specific group, can a member who refuses to participate be compelled? The text presents two Hanafi opinions based on analogical reasoning and the nature of harm. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists balance collective benefit with individual rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the canal type and basic obligation
The third type of canal benefits only specific individuals. The cost of digging is an obligation (wajib) on all those who benefit, not on the public.
This is uncontested; the disagreement is only about compulsion of a refuser.
2
State the first opinion and its analogy
One view holds that a refuser must be compelled, drawing analogy with the second type of canal (which had a public element). There, compulsion was allowed because general harm outweighed specific harm.
Analogy (qiyas) is used to extend the ruling from the second type to the third.
3
State the second opinion and its rationale
The other view holds that no compulsion is needed because both harms are specific and symmetrical. The refuser's share of expenses can be recovered financially, so compulsion is unnecessary.
This view emphasizes that the harm on the refuser (paying for something they may not want) is equal to the harm on others (loss of labour), so financial adjustment suffices.
4
Compare the bases of the two opinions
The key difference: the first opinion relies on analogy with a case where harm was asymmetric (specific vs. general); the second opinion focuses on the symmetry of harm and the possibility of compensation.
The second opinion is more consistent with the principle that specific harm can be addressed by monetary compensation.
5
Determine the preferred (mufta bihi) opinion
The commentary hints that the second opinion is preferred because it avoids compulsion and allows a fair financial solution. This aligns with later Hanafi fatwa practice.
Some later texts may cite this as the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ اور بہر حال تیسری قسم اور وہ ہر طرح سے خاص ہے تو اس کا کھودنا اس کے اہل کے ذمہ ہے اسی دلیل کی وجہ سے جو کہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں پھر کہا گیا ہے کہ انکار کرنے والے کو مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ جیسے دوسری میں اور کہا گیا ہے کہ مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ دونوں ضرروں میں سے ہر ایک خاص ہے اور ممکن ہے اس کا دور کرنا ان سے انکار کرنے والے پر رجوع کرنے کے ساتھ اس مال کے سلسلہ میں جو انھوں نے اس میں خرچ کیا ہے جبکہ خرچ کرنا قاضی کے حکم سے ہو تو دونوں جانب برابر ہو گئیں بخلاف اس صورت کے جو مقدم ہوتی ہے۔ تشریح تیسری قسم کی نہر بھی وہی لوگ کھو دیں گے جو اس سے نفع اٹھائیں گے اسی دلیل سے جو نہر ثانی کے بیان میں گذر چکی ہے ۔ پھر اگر کوئی انکار کرے تو اس پر جبر کیا جائے گا یا نہیں تو اس میں دو قول ہیں (۱) جبر ہو گا، کیوں ، جیسے ثانی میں جبر کیا گیا تھا (۲) جبر نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ دونوں ضرر خاص ہیں لہذ ا سابق دلیل یہاں جاری نہ ہوگی نیز ضرر کا دفعیہ اس طرح ہو سکتا ہے کہ یہ لوگ کھودیں اور اس کے حصہ میں جوصرفہ، بیٹھتا ہو وہ اس سے وصول کر لیں مگر یہ وصول کرنا جب درست ہوگا جبکہ انہوں نے جو خرچ کیا ہے وہ خرچ قاضی کے حکم سے ہو تو دونوں جانبین یہاں برابر ہیں ضرر کے خاص ہونے میں بھی اور خرچ لے کر دونوں ایک درجہ میں ہونے کے اعتبار سے بھی رہا مسئلہ قسم ثانی کا تو وہاں ضرر خاص کے مقابلہ میں ضرر عام تھا اس لئے وہاں جبر کیا گیا تھا۔
And anyway, the third type, and it is specific in every respect, so its digging is upon those who benefit from it, due to the evidence we have already mentioned. Then it was said that the one who refuses will be compelled, like in the second type. And it was said that he will not be compelled, because both harms are specific, and it is possible to remove the harm by recourse to the refuser regarding the wealth they spent, provided the expenditure was by the judge's order. So the two sides become equal, unlike the preceding case. Commentary: The third type of canal will also be dug by those who benefit from it, due to the same evidence mentioned in the discussion of the second type. Then if someone refuses, as to whether he will be compelled, there are two opinions: (1) He will be compelled, as was done in the second type. (2) He will not be compelled because both harms are specific, so the previous evidence does not apply here. Also, the harm can be removed by these people digging and recovering their expenses from the refuser's share, provided the expenditure was ordered by the judge. So the two sides become equal, both in terms of specific harm and in the ability to recover costs. The case of the second type was different because there specific harm was compared to general harm, so compulsion was applied.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on digging a canal that benefits only a specific group. The author identifies it as the third type of canal, where all users are specific. The ruling is that the cost of digging is the responsibility of those who benefit. However, there is a disagreement on whether those who refuse to participate can be compelled. One view supports compulsion by analogy with the second type (where the benefit is mostly specific with some public). The other view argues against compulsion because both harms are specific, and it is possible to compensate the refuser financially. The passage provides clear reasoning based on the nature of harm and the possibility of financial adjustment.

Key Terms
قسم ثانی (qism thani): The second type of canal, where benefit is mostly specific but with some public interest.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion by the authorities to participate in digging the canal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to one view in the passage, why is the one who refuses to dig a canal not compelled?
A) Because the canal is public property
B) Because both harms are specific and financial recourse is possible
C) Because the canal is for drinking only
D) Because the refuser is immune from compulsion

Key Takeaway
In a canal of specific benefit, the refuser is not compelled because both harms are specific and financial recourse is possible.

Cost Sharing for a Shared Canal: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cost sharing for digging a shared canal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on canal digging costs, explain their proofs, and apply the practical difference to a real scenario.

Core Idea
The cost of digging a shared canal: Abu Hanifa stops charging once water reaches your land; Sahibayn say you pay for the whole.

In a shared canal, landowners must contribute to digging costs. But when does each shareholder's obligation end? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, the Sahibayn, disagreed sharply. This lesson unpacks their arguments, the analogical reasoning each side uses, and the real-world difference in cost allocation.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: cost is lifted once digging passes each landowner's land. The Sahibayn's ruling: cost is shared by all from beginning to end proportionally.
Abu Hanifa focuses on individual benefit; Sahibayn on collective benefit from drainage.
2
Identify the legal reasoning of each
Abu Hanifa argues the purpose (irrigation) is achieved for the upper owner, so he is not obliged to benefit others. The Sahibayn argue the upper owner needs the lower part to drain surplus water, thus benefiting from the entire canal.
Each side uses a different conception of benefit.
3
Trace the qiyas used by Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa analogizes to a channel on another's roof: the roof owner maintains it, not the user. Similarly, the lower canal portion belongs to lower landowners to maintain.
The illa (effective cause) is that the benefit belongs to the property owner, not the one passing water.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's objection and Abu Hanifa's response
The Sahibayn argue that the upper owner has a right in the lower part for drainage. Abu Hanifa responds that the upper owner can block the water and is not compelled to use the lower part; the benefit is incidental.
Hanafi response highlights free choice and lack of compulsion.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a sample scenario
Take ten farmers with equal land sharing a canal. Under Abu Hanifa, the first farmer pays only for his portion; under Sahibayn, all pay equally for the entire length.
The practical difference is significant in cost distribution.

Source Text

کتاب احياء الموات
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴ تشریح وہ نہر خاص جو کہ مشترک ہے تو اس کی کھدائی اول سے شروع ہوگی اور صرفہ ان سب پر شرب اور زمینوں کے تناسب سے پڑے گا لیکن جن جن کی زمین سے کھدائی آگے بڑھتی جائے گی تو آگے ان کے اوپر صرفہ نہ ہوگا اور یہ امام صاحب کا مذہب ہے۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ صرفہ سب پر آخر تک چلے گا کیونکہ اسفل کے محتاج جس طرح اسفل فلمیں اعلی والا بھی ہے کیونکہ جو پانی فاضل بچے گا اس کو کہاں لے کر جائے گا۔ یقیناً اس کو بہانا پڑے گا اور مسیل اسفل ہے لہذا جس طرح اس کا حق اعلیٰ میں ہے اسفل میں بھی ہے۔ تبی ... بقول امام صاحب اس کی صورت یہ ہوگی کہ مثلاً دس آدمی ہیں اور اتفاق سے سب کی زمین برابر نے تو اعلیٰ والے کی زمین کا جو صرفہ ہو اس کے دس حصے کر لو اور ہر ایک پر ایک حصہ ڈال دو۔ اور جب کھدائی آگے بڑھے تو صرفہ کو نو حصوں پر تقسیم کرو اور اسی حساب سے آخر تک چلو۔
امام صاحب کی دلیل
وله ان المقصد من الكرى لانتفاع بالسقى وقد حصل لصاحب الاعلى فلايلزمه انفاع غيره وليس على صاحب المسيل عمارته كما اذا كان له مسيل على سطح غيره كيف وانه يمكنه دفع الماء من ارضه بسده من اعلاه
ترجمہ اور ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ نہر کھودنے کا مقصد سیرابی کا نفع حاصل کرنا ہے۔ اور انتفاع صاحب اعلیٰ کو حاصل ہو گیا تو اس کو اپنے غیر کو نفع پہنچانا لازم نہیں ہے اور صاحب مسیل پر مسیل کی تعمیر لازم نہیں ہے جیسا کہ جبکہ ہو اس کے لئے مسیل اپنے غیر کی چھت پر کیسے ہو سکتا ہے حالانکہ اس کو ممکن ہے اپنی زمین سے پانی دور کر دینا نہر کو روکنے کے ساتھ اس کے اوپر سے ہے۔ تشریح یہ امام صاحب کی دلیل ہے نہر کھودنے کا جو مقصد ہے وہ اس کے حق میں پورا ہو چکا ہے لہذا دوسروں کو نفع پہنچانا اس کے ذمہ واجب نہیں ہے اور صاحبین نے جو دلیل بیان کی ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ زیادہ سے زیادہ آپ یہ فرمائیں گے کہ اسفل اس کی مسیل یعنی پانی کی گذرگاہ ہے حالانکہ مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ صاحب میل پر واجب نہیں کہ وہ مسیل کی تعمیر کرے۔ مثلاً کسی کو کسی کی چھت پر پانی بہانے کا حق ہے یعنی کسی کی چھت دوسرے کی مسیل ہے تو چھت کی تعمیر چھت والے کا فریضہ ہے نہ کہ صاحب مسیل کا لہذا اسی پر اس کو قیاس کیا جائے پھر اگر زیادہ پانی ہو تو یہ کیا ضروری ہے کہ وہ اسفل ہی میں بہانا ضروری ہے نہر بھی بند کی جاسکتی ہے جیسا کہ عام طور سے ہوتا رہتا ہے۔
And the cost of digging the shared canal is upon them from its upper part. When it passes the land of a man, it is lifted from him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Sahibayn) said: it is upon all of them from its beginning to its end according to shares of irrigation and lands, because the owner of the upper part has a right in the lower part due to his need to drain the surplus water into it. Translation: And the cost of digging the shared canal will be upon them from the upper part; when digging passes someone's land, the cost is lifted from him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the Sahibayn said: the cost is upon all of them from beginning to end according to shares of irrigation and lands, because the upper owner has a right in the lower part due to his need to drain the surplus water. Commentary: This shared canal's digging will start from the beginning and the cost will be upon all according to shares of irrigation and lands. But as digging passes each person's land, the cost is lifted from him. That is Imam Abu Hanifa's madhhab. The Sahibayn say the cost will continue on all until the end, because as the lower needs the upper, the upper also needs the lower to drain surplus water. They have to drain it; the channel is the lower part, so the upper has a right in the lower part. For example, if ten people with equal land, divide the cost of the upper person's land into ten parts, each pays one. When digging moves forward, divide the cost into nine parts, and so on. And Abu Hanifa's proof: the purpose of digging is to obtain irrigation benefit; the upper owner has obtained it, so he is not obliged to benefit others. The owner of the channel is not responsible for its maintenance, as when someone has a channel on another's roof. How can he be required? Moreover, he can divert water from his land by blocking it from above. Commentary: This is the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa: the purpose of digging is fulfilled for him, so he is not obligated to benefit others. The Sahibayn's argument is that the lower is his channel (masil) for water passage. But the owner of the channel is not obliged to construct it. For example, if someone has the right to pass water over another's roof, the roof owner is responsible for maintenance, not the one using it. So this is analogous. Moreover, if there is excess water, it is not necessary that he must drain it through the lower; he can block the canal. This is common practice.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage details the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the distribution of costs for digging a shared canal. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that each landowner bears the cost only for the portion of the canal that benefits his own land. Once the digging passes his land, he is no longer responsible. The Sahibayn hold that all landowners share the cost equally from beginning to end, proportional to their irrigation rights and land area, because the upper landowner needs the lower part to drain surplus water. Imam Abu Hanifa supports his view with an analogy: the owner of a channel is not required to maintain another's property, just as someone with a right to pass water over another's roof is not responsible for repairing that roof. Furthermore, the upper owner can block the water if needed. The commentary explains these arguments and responds to the Sahibayn's reasoning.

Key Terms
شرب (shirb): Share of irrigation water right proportional to land.
مسیل (masil): A channel or passage for water flow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when is the cost of digging a shared canal lifted from a landowner?
A) After the entire canal is dug
B) After the digging passes his land
C) After all partners agree
D) After the canal reaches a public river

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation to contribute to a shared canal's digging costs ceases for a landowner once his land's irrigation need is satisfied, unless he is compelled to use the lower channel.

When Does Cost Sharing for Canal Digging End?
Ruling on when a partner's canal digging cost ends: intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the point at which a landowner's cost share for digging a shared canal ends according to the preferred Hanafi view and explain the intra-madhhab difference with Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Cost sharing ends when digging passes the landowner's property boundary, not the irrigation channel mouth.

In partnerships for digging irrigation canals, the cost is shared based on benefit. A dispute arises: when exactly does a particular landowner's obligation to pay cease? This passage from Al-Hidayah presents two views within the Hanafi school and declares one more correct. Understanding this difference prevents financial disputes among partners.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify the view attributed to Abu Hanifa: the cost is lifted when the digging passes the landowner's land boundary.
2
Identify the opposing view (Muhammad al-Shaybani)
Recognize the alternative view: the cost ends when the digging passes the mouth (fuhha) of the landowner's irrigation channel.
This view is narrated from Imam Muhammad.
3
Analyze the basis of each view
Abu Hanifa's view rests on the landowner's general right to open his channel anywhere on his land, tying benefit to the land itself. Muhammad's view ties benefit to the specific channel mouth.
The text says 'because he has the right to open the mouth' – this supports Abu Hanifa.
4
Apply the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the channel mouth is inside the land, under Abu Hanifa the cost ends later (at land boundary); under Muhammad it ends earlier (at the mouth).
5
Note the tarjih and reason
The author declares the first view (Abu Hanifa) as more correct (asahh) because the landowner's right over his land is comprehensive.
The preferred view is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خرچ کب تک رہے گا ثم انما يرفع عنه اذا جاوز ارضه كما ذكرنا وقيل اذا جاوز فوهة نهره وهو مروى عن محمد والاول اصح فوّهة من اعلاه وا لان لـه رایـ ترجمہ. پھر اس سے صرفہ اٹھا دیا جائے گا جبکہ کھدائی اس کی زمین سے تجاوز ہو جائے جیسا کہ ہم ذکر کر چکے ہیں اور کہا گیا ہے جبکہ تجاوز ہو جائے اس کی نالی کے دہانے سے اور یہ محمدؐ سے مروی ہے اور اول اصح ہے اس لئے کہ اس کو حق ہے دہانا کھولنے میں نہر بالائی اور نیچے کے حصہ میں۔
Then, the cost is lifted from him when it passes his land as we mentioned. And it was said when it passes the mouth of his irrigation channel, and this is narrated from Muhammad. And the first is more correct, because he has the right to open the mouth in the upper and lower part of the canal.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage clarifies the exact point at which a landowner's share of the digging cost is lifted. The primary view is that the cost ends when the digging passes the boundary of the landowner's property. A second view, attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, holds that the cost ends when the digging passes the point where the landowner's irrigation channel (fuhha) joins the main canal. The author states that the first view is more correct (asahh) because the landowner has the right to open his channel mouth anywhere along his property, making the land boundary the decisive point.

Key Terms
فوهة (fuhha): The mouth or opening of an irrigation channel where it connects to the main canal.
جاوز (jawaza): To pass or exceed a boundary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the digging reaches the end of the canal
B) When the digging passes the mouth of his irrigation channel
C) When the digging passes his land
D) When all partners agree

Key Takeaway
The more correct Hanafi view is that a landowner's obligation to share canal digging cost ends when the digging passes his land boundary, not the mouth of his irrigation channel.

When Does the Digging Responsibility End? An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on ending of digging responsibility and dispute on opening water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's position on when digging responsibility ends and evaluate the two opinions on opening water after the canal passes beyond one's land.

Core Idea
The digging responsibility ends only when the landowner's own land is fully irrigated, not when the canal simply reaches his property.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the book of reviving dead lands: a landowner digs a canal from a common river to irrigate his land. When does his obligation to maintain the digging cease? Imam Muhammad gives a nuanced answer that turns on the complete irrigation of his land. The passage also presents a dispute about opening water after the digging passes his land. We will analyze the two views and their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage concerns a landowner who digs a canal from a river to irrigate his land. When does his financial responsibility for digging (mūnat) end?
The key is understanding the scope of his obligation.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position
According to Imam Muhammad, the responsibility continues until his own land is fully irrigated, because he has the right to choose where to dig on his land. The criterion is the land, not the channel.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
3
Identify the dispute about opening water
When the digging extends beyond his land, does he have the right to open the water to irrigate his land? Two opinions are given.
The first view allows it; the second prevents it until partners finish.
4
Analyze the first opinion
He may open water because the digging has ended in his right (lāntihāʾ al-karī fī ḥaqqih).
This is the preferred view in the text.
5
Analyze the second opinion
He cannot open until his partners finish, to negate exclusivity (nafyan li ikhtiṣāṣihi).
This view is transmitted as 'qīla' (it is said) and is weaker.
6
Examine the exemption of drinkers
Those who only drink from the river (ahl al-shafa) are not required to share in digging costs because they are countless and followers.
Their right is derived from the main users.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح ایک شخص کی مثلاً ۲۰ ایکڑ زمین ہے اور اس نے دس ایکڑ پر آ کر نہر سے اپنی نالی نکالی تو کیا ابھی سے اس کے اوپر سے صرفہ دور ہو جائے گا یا آگے جو دس ایکڑ اور باقی ہے اس سے آگے بڑھ کر ۔ تو امام محمد سے مروی ہے کہ ہاں اس نے اپنی نالی موری بناتی ہے وہیں تک اس پر صرف ہوگا آگے نہ ہوگا مگر اس یہ ہے کہ جب تک اس کی پارٹی زمین ختم نہ ہوگی اس کے ذمہ صرفہ رہے گا کیونکہ اس کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ پانی زمین میں جہاں چاہے موری بنائے تو مدار موری یہ نہ رہا بلکہ زمین پر رہا۔
کھدائی جب اس کی زمین سے آگے تک پہنچ جائے تو پانی کھولنے کا حق ہے یا نہیں
فاذا جاوز الكرى ارضه حتى سقط عنه مونته قبل له ان يفتح الماء ليسقى ارضه لانتهاء الكرى في حقه وقيل ليس له ذلك مالم تفرغ شركاؤه نفيا لاختصاصه وليس على اهل الشفة من الكرى شئ لانهم لا يحصون ولانهم اتباع
ترجمہ ہیں جب کہ کھدائی اس کی زمیں متجاوز ہوگی۔ یہاں تک اس سے اس کا صرفہ ساقط ہو گیا تو کہا گیا ہے کہ اس کوحق ہے کہ وہ پانی کھول لے تا کہ اپنی زمین کو سیراب کرے اس کے حق میں کھدائی کے ختم ہو جانے کی وجہ سے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ اس کو یہ حق نہیں ہے کہ جب تک کہ اس کے شرکا ، فارغ نہ ہوں اس کے اختصاص کافی کی وجہ سے اور پینے والوں پر کھدائی میں سے کچھ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ وہ بے شمار ہیں اور اس لئے کہ وہ تابع ہیں۔ تشریح جب کھدائی اس کی زمین سے آگے پہنچ گئی تو کیا اس کو حق ہے کہ پانی کھولکر اپنا کھیت بھرے تو اس میں دو قول ہیں (1) کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ کھدائی اس کے حق میں ختم ہو چکی ہے (۲) نہیں کر سکتا تا کہ یہ شبہ نہ ہو کہ انتفاع کے ساتھ نہیں مخصوص ہے جو لوگ صرف پانی پیئں کیا وہ بھی کھو دیں گے تو فرمایا کہ نہیں اس لئے اول تو وہ مجہول ہیں ( کما ہو ظاہر ) اور دوسرے یہ کہ وہ اتباع ہیں اور حکم متبوع پر لگتا ہے نہ کہ تابع پر شامی ص ۲۸۴ ج ۵ پر ہے کہ صرفہ مالک پر پڑتا ہے جس کو بطریق اباحت حق پہنچتا ہے اس لئے کہ اوپر نہیں پڑتا ۔
Explanation: A person has, for example, 20 acres of land, and he came to 10 acres and drew his canal from the river. Then, does his responsibility (mūnat) fall away from now on from the [first] part, or does it extend beyond to the remaining 10 acres? It is narrated from Imam Muhammad that yes, when he constructs his canal channel (mūrī), the responsibility falls up to that point only, not beyond. But this is the case: as long as his own land is not exhausted, the responsibility remains upon him, because he has the option to make the channel anywhere in his land. So the criterion is not the channel but the land. When the digging reaches beyond his land and his maintenance (mūnat) falls away, it is said that he has the right to open the water to irrigate his land because the digging has ended in his right. And it is said that he does not have that right until his partners finish, to negate exclusivity. And there is no responsibility for the people of drinking (ahl al-shafa) regarding the digging, because they are countless and because they are followers. Explanation: When the digging reaches beyond his land, does he have the right to open water to fill his field? There are two views: (1) He can, because the digging has ended in his right. (2) He cannot, so that there be no suspicion that he is not exclusive in benefit. As for those who only drink, will they also dig? It was said no, because first they are unknown (as is apparent), and second they are followers, and the ruling applies to the followed, not the follower. Al-Shami, vol. 5, p. 284: The maintenance falls upon the owner who receives the right as permission, because it does not fall on the upstream.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the book of reviving dead lands (Iḥyāʾ al-Mawāt), specifically about the responsibility for digging a canal from a common river. The issue: a person owns 20 acres, and he digs a canal from the river to irrigate 10 acres. The question is when his liability (mūnat) for the digging ends. Imam Muhammad's position is that the liability persists as long as his own land is not fully irrigated, because he has the right to choose where to dig the channel on his land. The criterion is the land, not the specific channel. The passage then presents a dispute: when the digging goes beyond his land, does he have the right to open the water? Two opinions are given. The preferred view (according to the text) is that he may open water because the digging is complete for his portion. The opposing view holds that he must wait until his partners finish, to prevent exclusivity. Finally, the text exempts those who only drink from the river from any digging responsibility, because they are numerous followers. The commentary cites al-Shami (a later Hanafi authority) clarifying that the maintenance falls on the landowner who has a right of use (ibāḥa), not on upstream owners.

Key Terms
الكرى (al-karī): The act of digging a canal or channel for irrigation.
المؤنة (al-mūnat): The responsibility or maintenance cost for digging.
أهل الشفة (ahl al-shafa): Those who drink from the water (as opposed to irrigating).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, when does the responsibility (mūnat) for digging an irrigation canal end for a landowner?
A) When the canal reaches his land
B) When his own land is fully irrigated
C) When the canal reaches the end of his property
D) When the partners finish their digging

Key Takeaway
The responsibility for digging a canal ends when the landowner's own land is fully irrigated; after that, he may open water for his land even if the canal continues.

Water Rights as Independent Property: Istihsan over Qiyas
Ruling on claim of water rights without land, with proof by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on claiming water rights without land and analyze the use of istihsan to override qiyas.

Core Idea
Water rights can be claimed without land because istihsan recognizes their independent value, overriding strict qiyas.

This passage introduces a key Hanafi principle: a claim for water rights (sharb) can be valid even without accompanying land, using juristic preference (istihsan). The text contrasts this with strict analogy (qiyas), which would reject such a claim. Understanding why istihsan prevails here illuminates the Hanafi approach to property rights and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is whether a person can validly claim water rights (sharb) without owning the land associated with that water.
2
Consider the strict analogy (qiyas)
By qiyas, the claim would be invalid, like a Muslim's claim to own wine—since a Muslim cannot own wine, the claim is not heard. Similarly, water rights without land might be considered non-ownable.
The text explicitly compares to wine ownership.
3
Examine the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan overrides qiyas because water rights can be inherited separately from land, or a person may sell land while retaining water rights. Water rights are valuable and tradable, so claims should be entertained.
Istihsan is based on custom and practical necessity.
4
State the ruling
The claim of water rights without land is valid (sahih) by istihsan.
This is the mufta bihi position.
5
Appreciate the cross-madhhab dimension
Other schools may require land ownership for water rights claims, but Hanafis recognize independent water rights as property.
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih (disputed).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الدعوى والاختلاف والتصرف فيه
ويصح دعوى الشرب بغير ارض استحسانا لانه قد يملك بدون الارض ارثا وقد يبيع الارض ويبقى له شرب وهو مرغوب في الدعوى
ترجمہ اور شرب کا دعوی استحسانا بغیر زمین کے صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ شرب بطور میراث کے کبھی بغیر زمین کے مملوک ہوتا ہے اور کبھی آدمی زمین فروخت کر دیتا ہے اور اپنا شرب باقی رکھ لیتا ہے اور شرب مرغوب فیہ ہے تو اس میں دعوی صحیح ہے۔ تشریح بغیر زمین کے اگر کوئی شرب کا دعوی کرے تو قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ تھا کہ اس کا دعوی مسموع نہ ہو کیونکہ شرب بغیر زمین کے تملیک کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا جیسے اگر کوئی مسلمان شراب کا دعوی کرے تو قیاس کا دعوی کرے تو وہ مسموع ہو گا لیکن یہاں قیاس کو چھوڑ کر استحسان پر عمل کیا گیا ہے کہ کبھی بغیر زمین کے بھی شرب میراث میں مل سکتا ہے اور یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ آدمی زمین بیچ دے اور شرب روک لے لہذا اس کا دعویٰ مسموع ہوگا۔
Section on claims, disputes, and dispositions concerning water rights. And the claim of water rights without land is valid by istihsan, because it may be owned without land through inheritance, and one may sell the land and retain his water right, and it is desirable (marghūb) in claims. Translation: The claim of water rights (sharb) without land is valid by istihsan, because water rights are sometimes owned without land by inheritance, and sometimes a person sells the land and retains his water right, and water rights are desirable in claims, so the claim is valid. Explanation: If someone claims water rights without land, qiyas would require that his claim not be heard, because water rights without land cannot be owned in principle, like if a Muslim claims wine, by qiyas it would not be heard. But here qiyas is abandoned in favor of istihsan, because sometimes water rights can be obtained without land through inheritance, and it is also possible that a person sells the land and retains the water right, so his claim is heard.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (fasl) on claims, disputes, and dispositions regarding water rights. The first ruling is that a claim of water rights (sharb) without accompanying land is valid by istihsan. The author explains the reasoning: apparently, by strict analogy (qiyas), such a claim would not be admissible because water rights are typically attached to land; without land, they have no tangible locus. The analogy is made to a Muslim claiming wine — wine cannot be owned by a Muslim, so the claim is invalid. However, istihsan permits the claim because in reality, water rights can be owned independently: they may be inherited separately from land, or a person may sell his land while reserving the water right. Since water rights are valuable and desired (marghūb fīhi), the court entertains the claim. This reflects the Hanafi principle of istihsan overriding qiyas when there is a recognized customary or legal basis for the exception.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-sharb): Water right; the right to draw water for irrigation or drinking.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; abandoning strict analogy for a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because water rights can never exist without land
B) Because strict analogy would make it invalid, but reality shows they can be inherited or retained
C) Because the claimant must be a Muslim
D) Because water rights are not valuable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, water rights (sharb) are independent property rights that can be claimed even without land, based on istihsan overriding qiyas.

Burden of Proof in Water Easement Disputes
Ruling on burden of proof for water easements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the burden of proof when a canal flows through another's land and apply it by analogy to other easements such as gutters and passage rights.

Core Idea
Possession or actual flow creates a presumptive right; the burden shifts to the one who objects.

In Hanafi law, when a canal flows through another's land, the mere fact of its use establishes a strong presumption of right. This lesson explains the rule and who must prove what in a dispute, and how the same principle applies to gutters, outlets, and rights of passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factual scenarios
First, determine whether the canal is currently in the alleged owner's possession or actually flowing. Second, if it is not in his hand and not flowing.
Possession includes physical control or active flow.
2
Determine the burden of proof
If the canal is in possession/flowing, the owner's word is accepted without evidence; the landowner becomes the plaintiff and must prove the canal has no right. If not in possession, the claimant (canal owner) must prove ownership or a prescriptive right.
The key distinction: possession shifts the evidential burden.
3
Extend by analogy to other easements
The same rule applies to water outlets (masabb), roof gutters (mīzāb), and rights of passage in another's house. If these are currently in use, the user's word is taken; otherwise, the claimant must prove.
Analogy (qiyas) is based on the shared effective cause: usage and possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا كان نهر لرجل يجرى فى ارض غيره فاراد صاحب الارض ان لايجرى النهر في ارضه ترک علی حاله لانه مستعمل له باجراء مائه فعند الاختلاف يكون القول قوله فان لم يكن فى يده ولم يكن جاريا فعليه البينة ان هذا النهرله او انه قد كان له مجراة فى هذا النهر يسوقه الى ارضه ليسقيها فيقضى له لاثباته بالحجة ملكا له او حقا مستحقا فيه وعلى هذا المصب فى نهر او على سطح او الميزاب او الممشى في دار [؟] فح الاختلاف فيه ظيره فى الشرب
ترجمہ اور جب کسی شخص کی نہر اس کے غیر کی زمین میں بہتی ہو پس زمین والے نے ارادہ کیا کہ اس کی زمین میں نہر نہ ہے تو نہبر کو اس کے حال پر چھوڑ دیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ وہ اس کو استعمال کرنے والا ہے اس کے پانی کو جاری کرنے کے ساتھ تو اختلاف کے وقت اس کا قول معتبر ہوگا پس اگر نہر اس کے قبضہ میں نہ ہو اور نہ جاری ہو تو مدعی کے ذمہ بینہ ہے کہ یہ نہر اس کی ہے یا اس کو اس نہر میں پانی جاری کرنے کا حق تھا جس کو وہ اپنی زمین میں لے جائے تا کہ وہ اپنی زمین کو سیراب کرے تو نہر کا فیصلہ اس کے لئے کر دیا جائے گا اس کے ثابت کرنے کی وجہ سے اپنی ملک یا ایسا حق جو اس میں ثابت ہو اور اسی طرح کسی نہر میں یا کسی چھت پر پانی بہانے کا حق یا پر نالہ یا گذرگاہ اپنے غیر کے گھر میں تو ان میں اختلاف کا حکم شرب میں اختلاف کی نظیر ہے ۔ تشریح کسی شخص کی نہر کسی زمین میں جاری ہے زمین والا چاہتا ہے کہ یہ نہر میری زمین میں نہ چلے تو اگر نہر صاحب نہر کے قبضہ میں ہے یا وہ نہر جاری ہے تو بغیر بینہ کے اس کو اس حال پر چھوڑ دیا جائے گا جس حال پر ہے یعنی صاحب نہر کے حق میں فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا اور اگر کوئی قبضہ کی صورت ظاہر نہ ہو اور نہ نہر جاری ہو تو پھر مدعی کو بینہ سے ثابت کرنا ہوگا کہ یہ میری نہر ہے یا مجھے یہاں کو پانی بہانے کا حق تھا کہ میں اس کو اپنی زمین میں لیجا کر زمین سیراب کروں پھر اس کے لئے فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا بینہ سے ملکیت ثابت کر دی ہو تو اس کا فیصلہ ہوگا یا پانی بہانے کا حق ثابت کیا ہو تو اس کا فیصلہ ہوگا۔

And if there is a river belonging to a person flowing in the land of another, and the landowner intends that the river should not flow in his land, it is left as it is because he (the owner) is using it by letting his water flow. So in case of dispute, his word is taken. If it is not in his hand and not flowing, then the burden of proof is on the claimant that this river is his or that he had a right to a channel in this river to convey it to his land to irrigate it, then a judgement is given for him due to his establishing by proof ownership or a vested right. And similarly for the outlet into a river, or on a roof, or the gutter, or the passage in a house, the ruling on dispute is analogous to that in water rights. Translation: And when a person's canal flows in another's land, and the landowner wants that the canal should not be in his land, the canal is left as is because he (the owner) is the user by letting his water flow. So when there is a dispute, his statement is accepted. If the canal is not in his possession and not flowing, then the claimant must provide evidence that this canal is his or that he had a right to a channel in this canal to convey it to his land to irrigate it, then judgement is given for him based on establishing evidence of ownership or a right that is vested therein. And similarly, for the outlet into a canal, or on a roof, or the gutter, or the passage in another's house, the ruling on dispute is analogous to that mentioned in water rights. Explanation: If a person's canal is flowing in someone's land, and the landowner wants it to stop, then if the canal is in the possession of its owner or is flowing, then without evidence it is left as is, i.e., judgement is given in favor of the canal owner. But if there is no possession apparent and the canal is not flowing, then the claimant must prove by evidence that this is his canal or that he had a right to flow water here to irrigate his land. Then judgement will be given based on evidence of ownership or right. Similarly, if someone claims that he had the right to flow excess water in a certain canal, or on a certain roof, or his gutter flows into another's land, or he has a right of passage, then the same rule applies as mentioned for water rights: if these things currently exist, his word is accepted; otherwise he must prove.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for disputes over water channels (canals) flowing through another's land, and extends it to other easements. The key principle is: the current state (possession or actual flow) determines who holds the burden of proof. If the canal is in the alleged owner's possession or is actually flowing, the court presumes his right and his statement (that he has the right) is accepted without evidence. The landowner who objects becomes the plaintiff. Conversely, if the canal is not in his possession and not flowing, the claimant must prove ownership or a prescriptive right. The rule is then extended by analogy to other water-related rights (outlets, roof drainage, gutters) and even to rights of passage. This reflects the Hanafi maxim that possession and use establish a presumption of right.

Key Terms
المجراة (al-majarāh): The channel or conduit for water; the right to a watercourse.
المصب (al-maṣabb): The outlet where water discharges into a river or canal.
الميزاب (al-mīzāb): Gutter or spout for rainwater drainage from a roof.

Claim of Water Right Without Land: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling with proof on claim of water right without land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts a claim of water right without land by istihsan and state the qiyas counter-argument.

Core Idea
A water right can be owned independently of land, so a claim without land is valid by istihsan.

This lesson examines a ruling from the Book of Reviving Dead Lands. The Hanafi school faces a tension: strict analogy (qiyas) would reject a claim of water right without land, but istihsan (juristic preference) accepts it. Understanding why istihsan overrides qiyas here illuminates the Hanafi method of balancing textual precedent and practical need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is making a legal claim to a water right (sharb) without owning the associated land. The ruling: such a claim is valid (masmu') and will be heard by the judge.
2
Outline the qiyas argument
By strict analogy, the claim should not be heard because a water right without land is not normally subject to exclusive ownership (tamlik). The analogous case is a Muslim claiming wine, which is not owned by him.
3
Explain the istihsan argument
Istihsan overrides qiyas because water rights can be owned separately in practice: by inheritance without land, or by a seller retaining the right after selling the land. The water right is also a desirable thing (marghub fih).
4
Note the basis for preferring istihsan
The Hanafi school prefers istihsan here to avoid hardship and to recognize real-world transactions. The preference is based on specific instances of separate ownership.
5
Understand the practical consequence
Under Hanafi law, a person can litigate a water right independently, preserving valuable rights that would otherwise be lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب احياء الموات ترجمہ اور شرب کا دعوی استحسانا بغیر زمین کے صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ شرب بطور میراث کے کبھی بغیر زمین کے مملوک ہوتا ہے اور کبھی آدمی زمین فروخت کر دیتا ہے اور اپنا شرب باقی رکھ لیتا ہے اور شرب مرغوب فیہ ہے تو اس میں دعوی صحیح ہے۔ تشریح بغیر زمین کے اگر کوئی شرب کا دعوی کرے تو قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ تھا کہ اس کا دعوی مسموع نہ ہو کیونکہ شرب بغیر زمین کے تملیک کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا جیسے اگر کوئی مسلمان شراب کا دعوی کرے تو قیاس کا دعوی کرے تو وہ مسموع ہو گا لیکن یہاں قیاس کو چھوڑ کر استحسان پر عمل کیا گیا ہے کہ کبھی بغیر زمین کے بھی شرب میراث میں مل سکتا ہے اور یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ آدمی زمین بیچ دے اور شرب روک لے لہذا اس کا دعویٰ مسموع ہوگا۔
The Book of Reviving Dead Lands. Translation: A claim of water right without land is valid by istihsan, because the water right is sometimes owned without land by inheritance, and sometimes a person sells the land and retains his water right, and the water right is a desirable thing, so the claim is valid in it. Commentary: If someone claims a water right without land, the requirement of qiyas was that his claim should not be heard because a water right without land does not admit of ownership, like if a Muslim claims wine, by qiyas the claim would be heard. But here, leaving qiyas, istihsan is acted upon, because sometimes the water right is inherited without land, and it can also happen that a person sells the land and retains the water right. Hence his claim will be heard.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Reviving Dead Lands (Kitab Ihya al-Mawat). It addresses the validity of a legal claim to a water right (sharb) without accompanying land ownership. The author first states the ruling: such a claim is valid by istihsan. The proof is that a water right can be owned separately from land in two ways: by inheritance (where the water right may pass to heirs without the land) or by reservation (where a seller retains the water right after selling the land). Since the water right is a valuable benefit, the claim is accepted. The commentary explains the background: by strict analogy (qiyas), the claim would not be heard because water rights without land are not normally subject to exclusive ownership (tamlik), analogous to a Muslim claiming wine (which is not owned by him). However, the Hanafi school prefers istihsan over qiyas in this case because of the practical possibilities of separate ownership. This illustrates the Hanafi method of using istihsan to bring about ease and avoid hardship.

Key Terms
شرب (sharb): A right to water for irrigation, typically attached to land but here claimed separately.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that abandons strict analogy for a more suitable ruling.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning applied to derive a ruling for a new case based on an existing case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because water is a common resource and anyone can claim it
B) Because water rights can be inherited or reserved separately from land
C) Because qiyas is invalid in water disputes
D) Because the claimant always has proof

Key Takeaway
A claim of water right without land is valid by istihsan, because water rights can be owned independently through inheritance or sale conditions.

River Flowing Through Another's Land: The Role of Possession in Water Rights
Ruling with proof on river flow rights based on possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a currently enjoyed right (flowing river) is protected, and distinguish the requirement of evidence when the right is not in possession.

Core Idea
A river flowing through your land is protected by the owner's ongoing use; without possession, he must prove his right.

This passage addresses a common dispute: can a landowner stop a river that has been flowing through his land for someone else's benefit? The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between a right that is actively being exercised and one that is dormant. The key is possession (qabda) and active use. This lesson explains the two scenarios and the analogous extension to other rights like water discharge and passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct situations: first, where the river is in the possession of its owner or currently flowing; second, where it is not in possession and not flowing.
2
Understand the legal effect of each scenario
In the first scenario, the landowner cannot stop the river; the river is left as is. In the second, the claimant must provide legal evidence (bayyinah) to prove his right.
3
Grasp the underlying principle: istishab
The ruling relies on the presumption of continuity (istishab) – if a right is being exercised, it is presumed to continue until proven otherwise. This protects the current user.
istishab is a recognized usul principle in Hanafi law.
4
Extend the ruling to analogous rights
The same principle applies to the right of water discharge (masabb), roof drainage (mizab), and passage (mamarr). If these are currently in use, the user's statement is accepted; otherwise, evidence is required.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Some other schools of thought may give the landowner more authority to control water on his land. Recognize that the Hanafi position prioritizes established usage rights.

تنبیہ مجراہ حق اجراد، مصب ، ڈالنے کی جگہ یہاں مراد یہ ہے کہ اپنی ضرورت سے جو پانی فاضل ہو اس کو جہاں ڈالے گاوہ مصب ہے ( اگر کوئی نہر ہو تو وہی مصب ہوگی ) شامی صفحہ ۲۸۵ جلد ۵ مشی ، گذرگاہ، چلنے کی جگہ،،
If a person's river flows in another's land, and the landowner intends to stop it, what is the ruling? And if a river belonging to a man flows in another's land, and the landowner intends that the river not flow in his land, it is left as it is because he is using it by flowing its water. So in case of dispute, his statement is accepted. If it is not in his hand and not flowing, then the plaintiff must provide evidence that this river is his or that he had a channel in this river to take it to his land to irrigate it; then the river is decreed for him because he has established by proof ownership or a right. Similarly for the outlet into a river or onto a roof, or the spout, or the passage in someone's house; the ruling of dispute is analogous to that mentioned in water right. Commentary: If a person's river is flowing in someone's land, and the landowner wants it not to flow, then if the river is in the possession of the river owner or is flowing, without evidence it will be left as it is, i.e., decreed in favor of the river owner. If there is no possession and the river is not flowing, then the plaintiff must prove by evidence that this is his river or that he had a right to flow water here to irrigate his land; then it will be decreed for him. Similarly, if someone claims a right to discharge excess water into a certain river, or to flow water on a certain roof, or that his spout has a right to flow in someone's land, or that he has a right of passage in someone's land, then in all these cases the ruling is the same as mentioned for water right: if these things are already present, his statement is accepted without evidence; if not, he must prove. Note: 'Mujra' means the place of flow, 'masabb' means the place of discharge. Here it means that the surplus water from one's need is discharged somewhere; that place is the masabb. (If there is a river, that is the masabb.) Shami page 285 volume 5: 'mashi' means passage, 'guzargah' means walking place.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning a river (nahr) belonging to one person that flows through the land of another. The issue is whether the landowner can stop the river. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) If the river is in the possession of its owner or is currently flowing, the landowner cannot stop it; the river is left as it is, and in a dispute the owner's statement is accepted because he is actively using the water. (2) If the river is not in the owner's possession and not flowing, then the claimant must provide evidence (bayyinah) to prove that the river is his or that he previously had a right to channel water through that land. Once proven, the right is decreed for him. The same principle applies to other similar rights: the right to discharge surplus water into a river (masabb), the right to flow water on a roof (mizab), and the right of passage (mamarr) through another's property. The commentary clarifies that if these rights are already being exercised (i.e., existing on the ground), the claimant's statement is accepted without proof; otherwise, evidence is required. A note (tanbih) defines the terms: 'mujra' is the channel, 'masabb' is the outlet for surplus water.

Key Terms
نہر (nahr): A river or canal that carries water, which can be privately owned.
قبضہ (qabda): Possession or physical control; a key factor in determining who has the right without further evidence.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses, required to prove a claim when possession is absent.
مصب (masabb): The place where surplus water is discharged, e.g., into a river.
میزاب (mizab): Spout or drainpipe for rainwater.
ممر (mamarr): Passage or right of way through another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required of a claimant who sues for a water right (sharb) that is not currently in his possession or flowing?
A) He must take an oath
B) He must provide evidence (bayyinah)
C) He must pay compensation
D) He must wait one year

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi water rights law, possession and continuous use create a protected right; without them, proof is required.

The Prohibition of Building a Bridge on a Shared Canal Without Consent
Ruling on building a bridge on a shared canal without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building a bridge on a shared canal and apply the qiyas to a shared private road.

Core Idea
A shared canal is like a private road: no partner can alter it without unanimous consent.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a common property dispute: one partner building a bridge over a shared canal. The Hanafi ruling prohibits this without the consent of all partners. The reasoning is based on qiyas to a private path (tariq khas) shared among a group. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling and the analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is building a bridge (jisr) on a canal shared by a specific group. The ruling: it is not allowed (haram) without the consent of all partners.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The matn states the prohibition directly. The commentary expands using qiyas to a private road (tariq khas).
3
Trace the qiyas structure
Asl: shared private road (tariq khas) – no partner may alter it without consent. Hukm: prohibition of unilateral change. Illa: both are shared resources with defined rights. Far': shared canal. Therefore, the same prohibition applies.
The illa is jaliyyah (evident), extracted by tahqiq al-manat.
4
Note the exception
If all partners consent, the bridge becomes permissible. This is implied by the nature of shared ownership.
5
Identify the cross-madhhab nuance
Some other schools may permit the bridge if it causes no harm. Hanafi requires consent regardless of harm, emphasizing property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتخذ عليه جسرا ولا قنطرة بل هو بمنزلة طريق خاص بين قوم
ترجمہ: اور نہ بنائے اس پر پل کسی قوم کے درمیان طریق خاص کے درجہ میں۔
تشریح: اسی طرح کسی شریک کو حق نہیں ہے کہ نہر مخصوص مشترک پر لکڑی وغیرہ کا یا پختہ پل بنائے جیسے وہ راستہ جو چند آدمیوں کے درمیان مشترک ہو ان میں سے کسی کو یہ حق نہیں کہ راستہ میں تصرف کرے ایسے ہی یہاں البتہ اگر شرکاء راضی ہوں تو جائز ہے۔
And no one shall build upon it a bridge or an arch; it is like a private road among a people. Translation: And do not build on it a bridge, in the rank of a special path between a people. Commentary: Similarly, no partner has the right to build a wooden or masonry bridge on the common canal, just as on a path shared among several people, none of them has the right to make changes, except if the partners consent.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Hidayah regarding a canal shared among a limited group of people. The ruling establishes that no single partner may construct a bridge or any structure over the canal without the consent of all partners, because the canal is analogous to a private road (tariq khas) shared among a specific community. In Hanafi fiqh, just as no individual can alter a shared road without unanimous permission, here too any construction that affects the common resource requires collective agreement. The commentary clarifies that the prohibition applies whether the bridge is made of wood or masonry. However, if all partners agree, it becomes permissible.

Key Terms
جسر (jisr): A bridge built over a canal for crossing
طريق خاص (tariq khas): A private path shared by a specific group of people, not public

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No, because the canal is like a private road requiring unanimous consent
D) No, but only if the bridge is made of wood

Key Takeaway
Partners in a shared canal must obtain unanimous consent before building any structure.
