Private Canal from Shared Canal: Bridge Allowed
Exception for private canal owner's rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building or demolishing a bridge on a private canal derived from a shared canal with permission, and explain the rationale of pure ownership without harm.

Core Idea
A private canal derived with permission becomes pure property; the owner may build or demolish structures as long as water rights are unaffected.

This passage clarifies a specific exception to the general rule of shared water rights. When a person obtains partners' consent to create a private branch from a common canal, that branch becomes his exclusive property. Consequently, he has full discretion to construct or remove a bridge on his own canal, provided his actions do not increase his water intake beyond his entitlement. The key principle is that pure ownership (khalis milk) allows full disposition unless harm results.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act
The passage discusses a person who obtains permission to derive a private canal from a shared canal and then wants to build or demolish a bridge on it.
2
Determine the legal status
The act is permissible (mubah) because it involves pure ownership with no harm to others.
3
Understand the rationale
The person is disposing in his pure ownership (khalis milk) and the water intake does not increase, so no harm occurs to the partners.
The key phrase is 'tasarrut fi khalis milk' (disposition in pure ownership).
4
Recognize the condition
This ruling applies only when the private canal was derived with permission. Without permission, the canal remains shared and the default ruling applies.
5
Compare to the default rule
Typically, one cannot alter shared canals without partners' consent. This is an exception when the branch is privately owned.
The text says 'contrary to the case' indicating an exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف ما اذا كان لواحد نهر خاص يأخذ من نهر خاص بين قوم فاراد ان يقنطر عليه ويستوثق منه له ذلك اوکان مقنطرا مستوثقا فاراد ان ينقض ذلك ولا يزيد ذلك فى اخذ الماء حيث يكون له ذلك لانه يتصرف فى خالص ملكه وضعا ورفعا ولا ضرر بالشركاء باخذ زيادة الماء
ترجمہ: بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ کسی کی خاص نہر ہو جو کسی قوم کے درمیان خاص نہر سے لی ہے پس اس نے ارادہ کیا کہ اس پر پل بنا اور اس کو مضبوط کرے تو اس کو اس کا حق ہے یا وہ مضبوط پل تھا تو اس نے اس کو توڑنے کا ارادہ کیا اور یہ پانی لینے میں اضافہ نہیں کرتا تو اس کو اس کا حق ہوا اسلئے کہ وہ خاص اپنی ملکیت میں تصرف کرتا ہے باعتبار بنانے اور اکھاڑنے کے اور شرکاء کا کوئی ضرر نہیں پانی کی زیاتی کو لینے میں۔
تشریح: زید نے ایک مشترک خاص نہر سے ان کی اجازت سے ایک نہر نکالی اب وہ اپنی نہر پر پل بنانا چاہتا ہے تو چونکہ اس میں کسی کا کوئی نقصان نہیں ہے تو یہ جائز ہے یا پہلے سے اس پر پل تھا اس کو ہٹانا چاہتا ہے تو بھی جائز ہے کیونکہ اس میں کسی کا کوئی نقصان نہیں ہے وہ اپنی ملک میں تصرف کر رہا ہے جبکہ ایسا کرنے میں پانی کی آمد میں کوئی اضافہ نہیں ہوتا لہذا وضع اور بنانا (نفع اٹھانا) میں اس کو اختیار ہے۔
Contrary to the case when someone has a private canal taken from a specific canal among a people, and he intends to build a bridge upon it and strengthen it, he has that right; or if it was an already strengthened bridge and he intends to demolish it, and this does not increase the taking of water, then he has that right because he disposes in his pure ownership both in building and removing, and there is no harm to the partners in taking extra water. Commentary: Zaid derived a canal from a shared specific canal with their permission; now if he wants to build a bridge on his own canal, since there is no harm to anyone, it is permissible; if there was already a bridge and he wants to remove it, that is also permissible because he is disposing in his own property, and his action does not increase the inflow of water; therefore in building and benefiting he has authority.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage provides an exception to the previous ruling. It describes a scenario where a person has obtained permission from the other partners to derive a private canal from the shared canal. In such a case, the person owns that canal exclusively. Therefore, he has the right to build or demolish a bridge on his own canal as he wishes, provided that his actions do not increase his share of water intake beyond his entitlement. The commentary uses the example of Zaid who, with permission, takes a branch from the common canal; his actions on his private branch do not harm the other partners, so he is free to construct or remove structures. The key principle is 'tasarrut fi khalis milk' (disposition in pure ownership): since the branch is his sole property, his control over it is absolute as long as it does not affect the rights of others.

Key Terms
نهر خاص (nahr khas): A canal that is exclusively owned by one person, even if derived from a shared source
خالص ملك (khalis milk): Pure ownership, free from the rights of others

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a person takes a private canal from a shared canal with the partners' permission, what is the ruling on building a bridge on that private canal?
A) Not allowed because the water source is shared
B) Allowed only if the partners agree again
C) Allowed, as long as it does not increase water intake beyond his right
D) Not allowed under any circumstance

Key Takeaway
A person who derives a private canal from a shared canal with permission owns it absolutely and may build or demolish structures as long as his actions do not harm the partners by increasing his water intake.

Prohibition of Widening the Canal Mouth
Ruling with talil on widening a shared canal mouth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on widening a shared canal's mouth and explain the two-fold talil (reasoning) that makes it haram.

Core Idea
Widening a canal's mouth is haram because it damages shared property and steals water.

You may think that since a partner's own canal is private, any modification is permissible. But Hanafi law draws a sharp line at the point of connection to the shared source. Even if other minor adjustments are allowed, widening the mouth is strictly prohibited. Let's examine why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled upon
The act is 'tawsi' fam al-nahr' — widening the opening of one's canal where it meets the shared water source.
This is distinct from other modifications like building a bridge on a private branch, which may be allowed.
2
State the ruling (hukm)
The ruling is absolute prohibition (haram). The partner is to be prevented (yumna') from doing it.
The ruling is enforced by the judge or authority.
3
Analyze the talil (reasoning)
Two independent harms are cited: (1) breaking the bank (kasr al-daffa) which damages the physical infrastructure of the canal, (2) increasing the water intake beyond one's rightful share, which harms other partners.
Either harm alone suffices to prohibit; together they strengthen the prohibition.
4
Recognize the legal characterization
The commentary labels the actor as 'ghasib' (usurper) because he is destroying others' rights. This places the act under the prohibition of ghasb, which is a major sin.
Ghasb in Hanafi fiqh is a prohibited act that requires restitution.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Any unilateral modification at the point of intake from a shared resource that increases one's share or damages the common structure is analogously prohibited.
This is a qiyas based on the illa of harm and excessive taking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمنع من أن يوسع فم النهر لانه يكسر ضفة النهر ويزيد على مقدار حقه في اخذ الماء
ترجمہ: اور اس کو منع کیا جائے گا نہر کا منہ کشادہ کرنے سے اس لئے کہ وہ توڑے گا نہر کا کنارا اور اضافہ کرے گا اپنے حق کی مقدار پر پانی لینے میں۔
تشریح: یعنی مذکورہ اشیاء سے تو اس کو روکا نہیں گیا تھا لیکن اگر وہ اپنی نہر کا منہ چوڑا کرنا چاہے تو اس سے اس کو منع کیا جائے گا کیونکہ وہ غاصب ہے دوسروں کے حقوق ضائع کرنے والا ہے۔
And he will be prevented from widening the mouth of the canal because he will break the bank of the canal and increase his right in taking water. Translation: And he will be prevented from widening the mouth of the canal because he will break the bank of the canal and increase his right in taking water. Commentary: He was not prevented from the aforementioned things, but if he wants to widen the mouth of his canal, he will be prevented because he is a usurper and destroyer of others' rights.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that even though a partner may be allowed to make certain modifications to his own canal (like building a bridge on a private branch), he is absolutely prohibited from widening the mouth of the canal where it meets the shared source. The reason given is twofold: first, widening would damage the bank (daffa) of the canal, causing structural harm to the common resource; second, it would allow him to draw more water than his rightful share, thereby harming other partners. The commentary strongly characterizes such an act as 'ghasib' (usurpation) because it violates the rights of others. This ruling applies universally: no partner may unilaterally enlarge his intake, even if his own canal is private, because the mouth is the point of connection to the shared water.

Key Terms
يوسع فم النهر (yuwassi' fam al-nahr): Widening the opening of the canal where water enters from the shared source

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Partition by Holes: Same Ruling as Widening
Extending the prohibition of widening to holes in a canal partition system

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the analogical reasoning (qiyas) from the prohibition of widening the canal mouth to the case of widening a hole (kuwwa) in a water partition system, and state the Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
Widening a hole in a shared water partition is prohibited, just like widening the canal mouth—both increase one's share unfairly.

In this concise passage from Al-Hidayah, the author extends a well-known prohibition to a practical scenario: water distribution among partners via holes (kuwah). The matn clearly states that just as no partner may widen the main intake of a shared canal, no partner may enlarge his individual hole. This extension is a textbook example of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi fiqh, where the effective cause (illa) is the unjust increase of water share.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling
Recall that in the preceding passage, the ruling was established: it is prohibited (haram) for a partner to widen the mouth of a shared canal because it increases his water share at the expense of others.
This original ruling serves as the 'asl' in the qiyas.
2
Recognize the new case (far')
The new case is when water is distributed through individual holes (kuwah) in the canal wall, each hole leading to a partner's private channel.
The kuwwa is the specific opening for one partner.
3
Extract the effective cause (illa)
The illa in the original case is 'increasing the water intake beyond one's rightful share'. The same illa exists in widening a hole—it allows more water to flow into one partner's channel, depriving others.
This illa is jaliyyah (clear) and directly observable.
4
Apply the analogy (qiyas)
Since the illa is present in both cases, the same ruling applies: widening a hole is also prohibited (haram). The text states 'kadha' (likewise) to indicate this analogical extension.
No partner has the authority (la yakunu lahu) to widen his hole.
5
Conclude the ruling
Thus, the Hanafi position is that any unilateral enlargement of a kuwwa in a partitioned water system is impermissible. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is prohibition.
This protects the rights of all partners and maintains equity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تشریح: نہر کا ان کے درمیان بنوارہ موریوں کے ذریعہ سے ہوا تھا جیسے ہمارے یہاں ہے تو کسی کو یہ اختیار نہیں ہے کہ اپنی موری کشادہ کرے۔
Likewise when the division is by holes. Translation: And similarly when the division is through holes. Commentary: The canal's division among them was through holes, as is our practice, so no one has the authority to widen his hole.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling on widening the canal mouth to the case where water distribution among partners is done through holes (kuwah) — openings at specific levels that allow water to flow into each partner's private channel. The matn asserts that the same prohibition applies: no partner may enlarge his hole. The commentary notes that this is the common practice (like in the author's locale). The reasoning is identical: widening a hole would allow more water than the partner's share, harming others. This is an example of qiyas (analogical reasoning) within the same category: just as widening the main intake is prohibited, so is widening any individual intake point.

Key Terms
كوة (kuwwa): A hole or opening in the canal wall through which water flows to a partner's channel

Moving a Hole Backward from the Canal Mouth: Prohibition
Ruling on moving a hole back from the canal mouth: prohibited due to increased water intake.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits a partner from moving his intake hole backward from the canal mouth and identify the reason (ihtibas al-ma') that invalidates the action.

Core Idea
Moving a hole back increases water detention and flow, giving more water than one's right — prohibited.

In this passage, the Hanafi text addresses a subtle tactic a partner might use to increase his water intake: moving his hole further inside the canal rather than at the mouth. At first glance, the hole size remains unchanged, so one might think it is allowed. However, the text reveals that the detention of water (ihtibas) in the larger canal section before the hole creates pressure that forces more water into his channel. This results in taking water beyond his legal right, and therefore the action is prohibited. We will walk through the reasoning and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action and its ruling
What is the partner trying to do? Move the hole back four cubits from the mouth. The ruling: he does not have the right (la yajuuz), i.e., prohibited.
2
Recognize the key factor: ihtibas al-ma'
The text states that by moving the hole back, water becomes detained (ihtibas) in the section before the hole, increasing the force and inflow.
Ihtibas means detention or accumulation.
3
Connect ihtibas to the legal consequence
Because the water detention increases inflow, the partner ends up receiving more water than his rightful share. This is the core of the prohibition.
4
Apply the general principle
The Hanafi principle that any change in the system that leads to taking more than one's share without consent is prohibited (haram). This ruling is a specific application of that principle.
5
Note the practical implication
Even though the hole size is unchanged, the increased flow due to detention is considered an unjust increase, and thus not allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا اذا اراد ان يؤخرها عن قم النهر فيجعلها في اربعة اذرع منه لاحتباس الماء فيه فيزداد دخول الماء
ترجمہ: اور ایسے ہی جبکہ ارادہ کیا کہ موری کو پیچھے ہٹائے نہر کے منہ سے پس اس کو کر دے نہر کے منہ سے چار ہاتھ پیچھے (اس کو اسکا حق نہ ہوگا) اس میں پانی کے محبوس ہونے کی وجہ سے پس بڑھ جائے گا پانی کا دخول۔
تشریح: کوئی شریک اپنے تختہ کو نہر کے منہ سے چار ہاتھ پیچھے لگانا چاہتا ہے تو اس کو اس کا حق نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس صورت میں اس میں پانی کی رفتار میں اضافہ ہوگا اور وہ اپنے حق سے زیادہ لینے والا ہوگا۔

Translation: And similarly when he intended to move the hole back from the mouth of the canal and place it four cubits behind (he will not have the right) because water becomes detained, so the inflow increases. Commentary: If a partner wants to move his plank four cubits back from the canal mouth, he does not have the right because in that case the water flow increases and he will take more than his share.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage addresses another subtle way a partner might try to increase his water share: by moving his intake hole further back from the canal mouth. By placing the hole at a distance, water accumulates (ihtibas) in the section before the hole, creating pressure that forces more water into his channel. The matn gives an example of moving it four cubits (dhirā') back. The commentary explicitly states that this action increases the force of water flow, resulting in the partner taking water beyond his right. Therefore, it is not allowed. The ruling is based on the principle that any change that increases one's share without consent is prohibited, even if the hole itself is not widened.

Key Terms
احتباس الماء (ihtibas al-ma'): Detention or accumulation of water, causing increased pressure and flow

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it changes the shape of the hole

Key Takeaway

Vertical Adjustment of Water Holes: Permissible
Ruling on vertical adjustment of water holes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vertical adjustment of a water intake hole and explain the legal reasoning (talil) based on the definition of water division.

Core Idea
Vertical change does not alter water share—only width matters.

This passage clarifies an important exception in Hanafi water distribution law. While partners may not alter their water intake in ways that change the division, vertical adjustment (raising or lowering) is permissible because the water share is determined by the hole's horizontal cross-section, not its height. Understanding this distinction prevents unwarranted restrictions on a partner's right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage addresses a partner raising or lowering his water intake hole. The ruling is that this is permissible (ja'iz) in the correct opinion (al-sahih).
This is an exception to the general prohibition on altering the hole.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the legal reasoning (talil): water division is fundamentally based on the width (s'ah) and narrowness (dīq) of the hole, not its vertical position.
The text states 'lianna qismat al-mā' fi al-aṣl bi i'tibār sa'at al-kuwwa wa dīqiha'.
3
Understand the talil: why vertical movement is not a change
Vertical adjustment does not alter the width or narrowness of the hole; it only changes its height. Therefore, it does not affect the water division because the division is based on horizontal area.
Custom ('ādah) also supports this: vertical changes are not considered a change in division place.
4
Contrast with prohibited actions
Horizontal movement (forward/backward) or resizing the hole (widening/narrowing) would change the place of division or the water share, and are prohibited.
This helps reinforce the scope of the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف ما اذا اراد ان يسفل كواه اويرفعها حيث يكون له ذلك في الصحيح لان قسمة الماء في الاصل باعتبار سعة الكوة وضيقها من غير اعتبار التسفل والترفع هو العادة فلم يكن فيه تغيير موضع القسمة
ترجمہ: بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ ارادہ کیا اس نے کہ اپنی موری کو نیچی یا اوپر کر دے اس حیثیت سے کہ صحیح قول کے مطابق اس کا اس کو حق ہے اس لئے کہ اصل میں پانی کا بٹوارہ موری کی کشادگی اور اس کی تنگی کے اعتبار سے ہے تسفل اور ترفع کا اعتبار کئے بغیر عادت یہی ہے تو اس میں قسمت کی جگہ کو بدلنا نہیں ہے۔
تشریح: شریک کی موری، جس جگہ ہے وہاں سے اس کو آگے پیچھے کئے بغیر اوپر یا نیچے کرنا چاہتا ہے تو یہ جائز ہے کیونکہ یہاں پانی کا بوارہ در اصل موری کی کشادگی اور تنگی کے مطابق ہے جس میں وہ کچھ اضافہ نہیں کر رہا ہے بلکہ جس جگہ ہے اس سے کچھ بلند یا نیچے کرنا چاہتا ہے تو اس میں بٹوارہ کی جگہ کا تغیر و تبدل نہیں ہے لہذا یہ جائز ہے۔
Contrary to when he intends to lower his hole or raise it, where he has that right according to the correct opinion, because fundamentally the division of water is based on the width and narrowness of the hole, without considering the lowering or raising, as is the custom; thus there is no change in the place of division. Commentary: If a partner wants to raise or lower his hole from its place without moving it forward or backward, it is permissible because the water division is based on the width and narrowness of the hole, which he is not changing; he is only moving it vertically from its spot, so there is no change in the division location, so it is permissible.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage provides an exception to the previous prohibition: a partner may adjust his hole vertically — either raising it or lowering it — as long as he does not move it forward or backward. The reasoning is that in Hanafi water distribution law, the division of water is determined by the horizontal cross-sectional area of the hole (s'at al-kuwwa wa dīqiha), not by its vertical position. Customarily, vertical adjustments are not considered to change the place of division (mawdi' al-qismah). Therefore, such modifications are permissible because they do not alter the actual amount of water allocated to the partner. The commentary emphasizes that the partner is not increasing the size of the hole; he is only changing its elevation, which does not affect the flow rate in the same way as horizontal movement would.

Key Terms
تسفل وترفع (tasafful wa taraffu'): Lowering and raising of a hole vertically

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because any change is prohibited
B) Yes, because it does not affect the water division based on width
C) Yes, but only if all partners agree
D) No, because it is like moving it backward

Key Takeaway
Vertical adjustment of a water hole is permissible because water division is based on width, not height.

Changing Water Division Method: Unilateral Change Not Allowed
Ruling on changing water division method from holes to days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that an established water division method cannot be changed unilaterally and explain the legal maxim that supports it.

Core Idea
An established water division system cannot be changed unilaterally; the old is left upon its state.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario in shared water rights: one partner wants to change the division method from holes (time-sliced by volume) to days (time-sliced by rotation). The ruling is clear: no unilateral change is allowed. The underlying legal maxim—'al-qadim yutraku 'ala zahirihi'—stabilises mutual agreements and prevents one party from disrupting established arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What action is being discussed? A partner intends to change the water division method from holes to days. The ruling: he does not have the right to do so.
The ruling is a prohibition (haram) because it is a unilateral change without consent.
2
Identify the legal principle
The proof cited is the maxim: 'al-qadim yutraku 'ala zahirihi' (the old is left upon its established state). Understand the meaning: the existing arrangement is preserved unless all parties agree otherwise.
This maxim is a foundational rule in Hanafi property law.
3
Apply the principle to the case
The division by holes is the 'qadim' (old system). It must remain as it is ('yutraku 'ala zahirihi'). Therefore, one partner cannot impose a change to days.
The ruling protects the stability of established practices and prevents unilateral disruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت القسمة وقعت بالكوى فاراد احدهم ان يقسم بالايام ليس له ذلك لان القديم يترك على ظـهـوره
ترجمہ: اور اگر بنوارہ موریوں کے ذریعہ واقع ہوا ہو پس ان میں سے ایک نے ارادہ کیا کہ ایام کے ذریعہ بٹوارہ ہو تو اس کو اس کا حق نہیں اس لئے کہ پرانی چیز کو اس کی اصلی حالت پر چھوڑا جاتا ہے۔
تشریح: موریوں کے ذریعہ بٹوارہ ہوا تھا اب کوئی شریک ایام کے ذریعہ تقسیم کا مطالبہ کرے تو اسے حق نہیں ہے کیونکہ جو تقسیم قائم ہے اس کو قائم رکھا جائے گا۔

Translation: And if the division occurred through holes, then one of them intended to have division by days, he does not have that right because the old is left upon its original state. Commentary: If the division was through holes, now one partner demands division by days, he does not have the right because the existing division will be maintained.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that once a method of water distribution has been established among partners - in this case, by holes (kuwah) - no single partner can demand a change to a different method, such as division by days (ayyam). The legal principle invoked is 'al-qadīm yutraku ʿalā ẓahūrihi', meaning 'the old is left upon its (established) state' or 'the previous status quo is preserved'. This is a manifestation of the Hanafi legal maxim that established practices (especially those based on mutual agreement or custom) are not to be altered unilaterally. The commentary stresses that the existing division system will be maintained, implying that any change requires unanimous consent of all partners. The ruling protects stability and prevents one partner from imposing a new system that might benefit him at the expense of others.

Key Terms

الكوى (al-kuwā): Holes or sluice gates used as a method to divide water by volume or time; a specific method of irrigation division
الأيام (al-ayyām): Days; a method of dividing water by allocating specific days to each partner

Key Takeaway

Partner Closing Openings in Shared Channel
Ruling on closing openings in a shared water channel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one partner closes openings in a shared private canal and explain the legal basis (prohibition of harm).

Core Idea
One partner may not close shared canal openings unilaterally because it harms the other; consent is required.

In the Hanafi school, the principle 'no harm and no reciprocating harm' (la darar wa la dirar) governs many property disputes. This lesson examines a specific case from Al-Hidayah: a partner wanting to close openings in a shared private canal to protect his land. The ruling clarifies that such unilateral action is prohibited because it damages the other partner's water rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the passage to identify the specific scenario: two partners share a private canal with multiple openings. One wants to close some openings to prevent water damage to his land. State the ruling: he has no right.
2
Extract the legal basis
The prohibition is based on causing harm (darar) to the partner. Harm is prohibited in Islamic law.
Principle: la darar wa la dirar.
3
Recognize the analogy
The passage compares closing openings to a disposition (tasarruf) in shared property without consent, which is not allowed. It also states that just as widening an opening requires consent, so does closing one.
Analogous to not being able to widen an opening without consent.
4
Apply to similar cases
Consider other modifications to shared water channels: increasing or decreasing flow requires mutual agreement.
This is consistent with the Hanafi position on joint ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اراد الاعلى من الشريكين فى النهر الخاص وفيه كوى بينهما ان يسد بعضها دفعا لفيض الماء عن ارضه کیلا تنزل [؟] بالاخر ترجمہ اور اگر ارادہ کیا شہر خاص میں دو شریکوں میں سے ایک نے اور اس میں ان کے درمیان موریاں ہیں یہ کہ ان میں سے بعض کو بند کر دے اپنی زمین سے پانی بہنے کو دور کرنے کے لئے تا کہ زمین نمناک نہ ہو جائے تو اس کو اس کا حق نہ ہوگا بوجہ اس کے کہ اس میں دوسرے کا ضرر ہے۔ تشریح مبسوط میں صورت مسئلہ یہ مذکور ہے کہ ایک بڑی نہر میں دو آدمیوں کے لئے پانچ موریاں لگی ہوتی ہیں ان میں سے ایک کی زمین او پر اور دوسرے کی نیچے ہے اب اوپر والا چاہتا ہے کہ ان موریوں میں سے ایک یا دو کو بند کر دے کیونکہ پانی کی کثرت کی وجہ سے زمین نمناک ہوتی ہے تو اس کو اس کا حق نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ مکان مشترک میں اپنے ساتھی کی رضامندی کے بغیر تصرف ہے تو یہ ایسے ہی جائز ہیں ہے جیسے موری کو کشادہ کرنے کا حق نہیں ہے۔
And if the higher partner in a private canal, and there are openings between them, intends to close some of them to prevent water from overflowing onto his land so that his land does not become damp, then he has no right to do so because it causes harm to the other. Translation: … Explanation: In al-Mabsut the case is mentioned that there is a large canal with five openings for two people, one's land is higher and the other's lower. The higher one wants to close one or two of these openings because excessive water makes the land damp, but he has no right because it is a disposition in shared property without the partner's consent. So it is not permissible, just as he has no right to widen an opening.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Al-Hidayah, commented upon in Ashraf al-Hidayah. The author states the case of two partners sharing a private canal (nahr khas) with several small openings (kuwa) for irrigation. The partner whose land is higher wants to close some of these openings to prevent water from seeping into his land and causing dampness. The ruling is that he may not do so unilaterally, because it would harm the lower partner by reducing his water supply. The legal basis is that shared property requires mutual consent for any alteration—here, closing an opening is like a disposal (tasarruf) in joint property without permission. This is analogous to the rule that one partner cannot expand an opening without consent. The 'illa (cause) is the harm (darar) caused to the other. The passage is an expansion of the matn, typical of sharh, and provides a concrete scenario from al-Mabsut.

Key Terms
النهر الخاص (al-nahr al-khas): A private canal or water channel owned by individuals, not public.
الكوى (al-kuwa): Small openings or sluices in a canal for irrigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is allowed because he is protecting his property.
B) It is not allowed without the consent of the lower partner.
C) It is allowed only if he compensates the lower partner.
D) It is obligatory to close them to prevent waste.

Key Takeaway
A partner in a shared private canal may not unilaterally close openings because it harms the other partner, which is prohibited in Hanafi fiqh.

Changing Water Division: Consent and Revocability
Ruling on changing water distribution method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on changing water distribution method in a shared canal and explain why the new arrangement is classified as a revocable loan (i'arah).

Core Idea
Changing water division method requires mutual consent; the agreement is a revocable loan, not a binding exchange.

In Hanafi fiqh, water rights (shurb) are not considered valuable property and cannot be sold or exchanged. This lesson examines a scenario where partners initially divide water through fixed openings and one proposes a time-based rotation. The text explains the legal classification of such an agreement and its revocability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial setup
Two partners share a canal, each with their own fixed openings (kuwa) for water intake. This is the established division method.
The openings represent a prior allocation of water rights.
2
Understand the proposed change
One partner proposes to change to a time-based rotation (e.g., alternating full use of the canal for a set number of days). The other partner may or may not consent.
3
Determine the default ruling
Unilateral change is not permissible (la yajuz) because the original division by openings is already established as a right.
The default ruling is that the existing method stands unless both agree to change.
4
Recognize the exception with mutual consent
If both partners agree, the change is permissible. However, the agreement is not a binding exchange; it is considered a loan (i'arah) of the water right because exchanging water for water is invalid.
The i'arah is revocable at the will of the lender (the partner who grants the change).
5
Identify the consequence of revocability
The lower partner (or his heirs) can unilaterally revoke the agreement at any time and revert to the original division by openings.
This upholds the principle that water rights are not permanently alienable.

Source Text

وكذا اذا اراد ان يقسم الشرب مناصفة بينهما لان القسمة بالكوى تقدمت الا ان يتراضيا لان الحق لهما وبعد التراضى لصاحب الاسفل ان ينقض ذلك وكذا الورثة من بعده لانه اعارة الشرب فان مبادلة الشرب بالشرب باطلة. ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی جبکہ ارادہ کیا ان میں سے ایک نے ) کہ شرب کو آدھا آدھا بانٹ لیں ( تو جائز نہیں ہے ) اس لئے کہ موریوں سے ہوارہ مقدم ہو چکا ہے مگر یہ کہ وہ دونوں راضی ہو جائیں اس لئے کہ حق ان دونوں کا ہے اور راضی ہونے کے بعد اسفل والے کو حق ہے کہ اس کو توڑ دے اور ایسے ہی اس کے ورثہ کو حق ہے اس کے بعد اس لئے کہ یہ اعارة شرب ہے اس لئے کہ شرب کا شرب سے تبادلہ باطل ہے۔ تشریح دو شریک ہیں ان کی ایک نہر ہے جس میں دونوں نے اپنی اپنی موریاں لگا رکھی ہیں اب اوپر والا کہتا ہے کہ باری اس طرح طے کر لیں آٹھ دن پوری نہر تم چلا ؤ اور آٹھ دن میں تو صاحب اعلیٰ کو اس کا حق نہیں ہے کیونکہ ہوارہ پہلے سے موریوں کے ذریعہ ہو چکا ہے البتہ اگر دوسرا شریک بھی راضی ہو تو جائز ہے۔ مگر شرب کا شرب سے تالہ تو باطل ہے تو اس کو اعارہ قرار دیا جائے گا اور اعادہ معیر جب چاہے رجوع کرے لہذ ا صاحب اسفل کو حق ہوگا کہ جب چاہے اس معاہدہ سے رجوع کرنے اور اس کے مرنے کے بعد اس کے ورثہ کا بھی یہ حق باقی رہے گا کہ جب چاہیں اس معاہدہ کو تو ڑ کر حسب سابق بیوارہ پر عمل کریں۔
And likewise if one of them wants to divide the water share (shurb) equally between them, it is not permissible because the division by openings has preceded, unless they both agree, because the right belongs to both. And after mutual consent, the lower owner has the right to revoke it, and likewise his heirs after him, because it is a loan of water right (i'arat al-shurb), since exchanging water with water is invalid. Translation: … Explanation: Two partners share a canal, each with their own openings. The higher partner says: let us fix a rotation – you take the whole canal for eight days and I for eight days. The higher owner has no right to this, because the division by openings has already been established. However, if the other partner agrees, it becomes permissible. But exchanging water for water is invalid, so it is considered a loan (i'arah). The lender (mu'ir) may revoke whenever he wishes, so the lower owner has the right to revoke this agreement whenever he wants, and after his death his heirs retain this right to break the agreement and return to the original division by openings.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where partners initially distribute water via fixed openings (kuwa). One partner (typically the higher landowner) wants to change to a time-based system (e.g., alternating full use of the canal in eight-day blocks). The ruling: such a change is not allowed unless both partners consent, because the original mode of division (by openings) is already established as a right. If both consent, the new arrangement is legally considered a loan (i'arah) of the water right, not a permanent exchange. This is because an exchange of water for water (mubadala) is invalid due to gharar and lack of mal mutaqawwim. Consequently, the lower partner (or his heirs) can unilaterally revoke the arrangement at any time and revert to the original division. This ruling highlights the Hanafi principle that established rights in water distribution are protected, and any change requires explicit consent and is revocable.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-shurb): The right to water for irrigation, a form of incorporeal right in Hanafi fiqh.
الإعارة (al-i'arah): A contract of loan for use, revocable at will by the lender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two partners in a canal agree to change from opening-based division to a time-based rotation, how is this agreement classified?
A) A binding contract of mubadala (exchange)
B) A revocable loan (i'arah) of the water right
C) A gift (hiba) of water shares
D) A sale (bay') of water rights

Key Takeaway
A change in water distribution method requires mutual consent and is revocable, not a binding exchange.

Water Rights (Shurb) in Hanafi Law: Inheritance, Bequest, and Invalid Contracts
Rulings on shurb regarding inheritance, bequest, and validity of contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water rights (shurb) regarding inheritance, bequest, and prohibited contracts, and explain the proof based on the absence of daman.

Core Idea
Water rights (shurb) are inherited and bequeathed as usufruct, but cannot be sold because they are not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim).

In this passage from a Hanafi commentary, we explore the unique legal status of water rights for irrigation. Unlike other schools that may treat water as property, the Hanafis distinguish between the right to use water (usufruct) and full ownership. This distinction has practical consequences: you can inherit a share of water, and you can will its use to someone, but you cannot sell, gift, or give it as charity. The reasoning is rooted in the concept of mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) and the evidentiary value of daman (financial liability).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal acts mentioned
The passage addresses inheritance (irth), bequest (wasiyya) of usufruct, sale (bay'), gift (hiba), charity (sadaqa), and bequest of these contracts. Note which are permitted and which are prohibited.
The bequest of usufruct (al-intifa' bi 'aynihi) is valid, but bequest of the shurb itself (ownership transfer) is invalid.
2
Extract the ruling for each act
Inheritance: valid. Bequest of usufruct: valid. Sale, gift, charity, and bequest of these contracts: invalid (bāṭil).
3
Analyze the reasons for invalidity
Three reasons are given: jahl (ignorance of water quantity), gharar (uncertainty), and that shurb is not mal mutaqawwim. The third is the key proof.
Mal mutaqawwim means property that can be sold and compensated if destroyed.
4
Examine the proof for not being mal mutaqawwim
The passage states: 'if someone irrigates from another's shurb, he is not liable (daman).' This absence of daman shows that shurb is not considered mal mutaqawwim, because if it were, unauthorized use would require compensation.
This proof is a qiyas (analogy) based on the principle that daman indicates property value.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a farmer who inherits a water right (shurb). He can use it, will its use to someone for a specific period, but cannot sell the right itself. If someone else irrigates without permission, the farmer cannot sue for financial compensation (daman), but can seek the ruler's discipline (ta'dib).
This highlights the unique nature of water rights as usufruct, not property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرب مما يورث ويوصى بالانتفاع بعينه بخلاف البيع والهبة والصدقة والوصية بذلك يعني بهذه العقود حيث لا تجوز العقود اما للجهالة او للغرر اولانه ليس بمال متقوم حتى لا يضمن اذا سقى من شرب غيره واذا بط الـ قود فالوصية بالباطل باطلة. ترجمہ اور شرب ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جو میراث ہوتا ہے اور اس کے عین سے انتفاع کی وصیت کی جاتی ہے بخلاف بیج ہبہ اور صدقہ کے اور بخلاف ان عقود کی وصیت کے کہ یہ عقود جائز نہ ہوں گے جہالت یا دھو کہ یا مال متقوم نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے یہاں تک کہ ضامن نہیں ہوتا اگر غیر کے شرب سے سیراب کرے اور جب یہ عقود باطل ہوئے تو باطل کی وصیت بھی باطل ہے۔ تشریح یہ ابتدائی مسئلہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے اور پہلے مسئلہ کی دلیل بھی ہو سکتی ہے۔ بہر حال شرب کے اندر میراث جاری ہوتی ہے اور اس کے عین سے انتفاع کی وصیت بھی جائز ہے کہ فلاں اس سے دو دن سیراب کرے گا اور باقی دنوں میں میرے ورثہ البتہ شرب کے نیچے کی وصیت کرنا باطل ہے کیونکہ جب شرب کی بیع اور ہبہ اور صدقہ کرنا کچھ بھی جائز نہیں بلکہ سب باطل ہے تو باطل کی وصیت بھی باطل ہے اور شرب کی بیع جائز نہ ہونے کی وجہ یا تو جہالت ہے یاد ھو کہ ہے کہ معلوم نہیں کتنا پانی آئے گا یا یہ ہے کہ یہ مال متقوم نہیں ہے اور مال متقوم نہ ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے دوسرے کے شرب سے اپنا کھیت سیراب کر لیا تو اس پر کچھ ضمان آتا تو جب یہ سب عقود ہوئے تو ان کی وصیت بھی باطل ہے۔ تنبیہ فقومی اسی پر ہے کہ مضمان واجب نہیں لیکن جس کے شرب سے سیراب کیا ہے اس کو چاہئے کہ مقدمہ بادشاہ تک لیجائے تا کہ اس کی کچھ تادیب کی جا سکے اور اگر اس نے چند مرتبہ ایسا کیا تو بادشاہ اس کی تادیب کرے پٹائی اور قید کے ذریعہ اگر اس میں مصلحت سمجھے ۔ مجمع الانہر ص ۵۴۳ ج ۲
And water rights (shurb) are among the things that are inherited and a bequest of usufruct of its substance is permitted, unlike sale, gift, charity, and a bequest of these contracts—these contracts are not valid either due to ignorance (jahl) or gharar or because it is not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), so much so that [if one] irrigates from another's shurb, he is not liable (daman). And when these contracts are void, a bequest of void things is void. Translation: … Explanation: This can be its own ruling or a proof for the previous ruling. In any case, shurb is inherited and a bequest of usufruct from its substance is valid—e.g., 'so-and-so shall irrigate for two days from it, and my heirs for the remaining days.' However, a bequest of the shurb itself (i.e., transferring ownership) is invalid because the sale, gift, and charity of shurb are all invalid. Therefore, a bequest of a void thing is void. The reason sale of shurb is invalid is either jahl (ignorance of quantity) or gharar, or because it is not mal mutaqawwim. The proof that it is not mal mutaqawwim: if someone irrigates from another's shurb, no compensation (daman) is owed (though the ruler may discipline him). Note: According to the jurists, daman is not obligatory, but the victim should take the matter to the ruler for discipline; if the offender repeats, the ruler may punish by beating or imprisonment if it serves the public good (Majma' al-Anhur).
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the legal nature of water rights (shurb). It begins by stating that shurb can be inherited (yurath) and a bequest of its usufruct (al-intifa' bi 'aynihi) is valid—meaning one can will that a specific person use the water for a set period. However, the shurb itself cannot be sold, gifted, given as charity, or bequeathed in a way that transfers ownership. The reasons given are: (1) jahl (ignorance) — the amount of water is uncertain; (2) gharar (deception) — the future flow is unknown; and (3) it is not mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) in Hanafi law, as proven by the fact that if someone irrigates from another's shurb, they are not liable for daman (financial compensation). This lack of daman distinguishes it from tangible property. The passage also notes that despite no legal liability, the victim may seek the ruler's intervention for ta'dib (disciplinary punishment) to deter repeated violations. This ruling underscores the unique status of water rights in Hanafi fiqh: they are protected rights of use but not commodities for sale.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-shurb): A right to water for irrigation, considered a usufruct right, not a property in itself.
المال المتقوم (al-mal al-mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is recognized in Islamic law and can be compensated if destroyed.
الجهالة (al-jahāla): Ignorance of the quantum or quality of the subject matter; invalidates a contract.
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract; prohibits sale of items with unknown future or quantity.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Financial liability or guarantee; compensation for damage or unauthorized use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of a water right (shurb) invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because water is free and cannot be owned.
B) Because it is not mal mutaqawwim and involves gharar.
C) Because only the government can distribute water.
D) Because it must be gifted, not sold.

Key Takeaway
Water rights (shurb) are inheritable usufruct but not saleable property in Hanafi law, due to uncertainty and being non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim).

Shurb: Inheritable and Bequeathable but Not Saleable
States the ruling on transferability of water rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the transferability of shurb (water rights) and explain why sale, gift, and charity are invalid while inheritance and bequest of usufruct are valid.

Core Idea
Shurb can be inherited and its usufruct bequeathed, but never sold, gifted, or given in charity—because it is not mal mutaqawwim.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we examine the unique legal status of shurb—the right to water for irrigation. Unlike land, shurb cannot be freely transferred through sale or gift, yet it passes to heirs and can be left as a usufruct in a will. Understanding why draws us into core Hanafi concepts of property (mal) and contractual validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transfers and their rulings
List the valid transfers (inheritance, bequest of usufruct) and the invalid ones (sale, gift, charity, bequest of ownership).
The passage states: 'yurith wa yusa bi al-intifa bi aynihi' vs 'al-bay wa al-hiba wa al-sadaqa'.
2
Extract the reasons for invalidity
Find the three reasons: jahl (ignorance), gharar (deceit/uncertainty), and not being mal mutaqawwim.
The text: 'li al-jahala aw li al-gharar aw li annahu laysa bi mal mutaqawwim'.
3
Understand the proof for not being mal mutaqawwim
Note the evidence: if someone irrigates from another's shurb, no financial compensation (daman) is due.
This shows shurb lacks the compensability that defines valuable property.
4
Distinguish between bequest of usufruct and bequest of ownership
Bequest of usufruct (e.g., 'irrigate for two days') is valid; bequest of the shurb itself (ownership) is void, following the invalidity of sale.
The text: 'al-wasiyya bi al-batil batila'.
5
Note the practical consequence
If someone uses another's shurb without permission, the ruler may discipline them, but no monetary compensation is due.
The warning (tanbih) from Majma' al-Anhar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب احياء الموات
والشرب مما يورث ويوصى بالانتفاع بعينه بخلاف البيع والهبة والصدقة والوصية بذلك يعني بهذه العقود حيث لا تجوز العقود اما للجهالة او للغرر اولانه ليس بمال متقوم حتى لا يضمن اذا سقى من شرب غيره واذا بط الـ قود فالوصية بالباطل باطلة.
ترجمہ اور شرب ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جو میراث ہوتا ہے اور اس کے عین سے انتفاع کی وصیت کی جاتی ہے بخلاف بیج ہبہ اور صدقہ کے اور بخلاف ان عقود کی وصیت کے کہ یہ عقود جائز نہ ہوں گے جہالت یا دھو کہ یا مال متقوم نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے یہاں تک کہ ضامن نہیں ہوتا اگر غیر کے شرب سے سیراب کرے اور جب یہ عقود باطل ہوئے تو باطل کی وصیت بھی باطل ہے۔ تشریح یہ ابتدائی مسئلہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے اور پہلے مسئلہ کی دلیل بھی ہو سکتی ہے۔ بہر حال شرب کے اندر میراث جاری ہوتی ہے اور اس کے عین سے انتفاع کی وصیت بھی جائز ہے کہ فلاں اس سے دو دن سیراب کرے گا اور باقی دنوں میں میرے ورثہ البتہ شرب کے نیچے کی وصیت کرنا باطل ہے کیونکہ جب شرب کی بیع اور ہبہ اور صدقہ کرنا کچھ بھی جائز نہیں بلکہ سب باطل ہے تو باطل کی وصیت بھی باطل ہے اور شرب کی بیع جائز نہ ہونے کی وجہ یا تو جہالت ہے یاد ھو کہ ہے کہ معلوم نہیں کتنا پانی آئے گا یا یہ ہے کہ یہ مال متقوم نہیں ہے اور مال متقوم نہ ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے دوسرے کے شرب سے اپنا کھیت سیراب کر لیا تو اس پر کچھ ضمان آتا تو جب یہ سب عقود ہوئے تو ان کی وصیت بھی باطل ہے۔
تنبیہ فقومی اسی پر ہے کہ مضمان واجب نہیں لیکن جس کے شرب سے سیراب کیا ہے اس کو چاہئے کہ مقدمہ بادشاہ تک لیجائے تا کہ اس کی کچھ تادیب کی جا سکے اور اگر اس نے چند مرتبہ ایسا کیا تو بادشاہ اس کی تادیب کرے پٹائی اور قید کے ذریعہ اگر اس میں مصلحت سمجھے ۔ مجمع الانہر ص ۵۴۳ ج ۲
And shurb is among those things that are inherited and for which bequest of usufruct is made, unlike sale, gift, charity, and bequest of those contracts, for these contracts are not valid due to ignorance or deceit or because it is not valuable property, to the extent that one is not liable if he irrigates from another's shurb, and when the contracts are void, the bequest of void is void. Explanation: This can be either an initial issue or a proof of the previous issue. In any case, inheritance applies to shurb and bequest of its usufruct is valid, e.g., 'So-and-so will irrigate from it for two days and for the remaining days my heirs.' However, bequest of shurb itself (as ownership) is void, because when sale, gift, and charity of shurb are not valid but all are void, then bequest of void is also void. The reason sale of shurb is not valid is either ignorance or deceit—it is unknown how much water will come—or because it is not valuable property. The proof it is not valuable property: if someone irrigates his field from another's shurb, no compensation is due. So when these contracts are void, their bequest is also void. Warning: The jurists hold that no compensation is obligatory, but the one whose shurb was used should take the matter to the ruler so that some discipline can be applied. If he does it repeatedly, the ruler should discipline him by beating and imprisonment if he sees fit. (Majma' al-Anhar, vol. 2, p. 543)
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the legal status of shurb (water rights/irrigation source). It establishes two categories: (1) shurb can be inherited and can be the subject of a bequest of usufruct (e.g., allowing someone to irrigate for a specified period); (2) shurb cannot be sold, gifted, given in charity, or bequeathed in ownership through those contracts. The reason for the invalidity of these contracts is threefold: ignorance (jahl) because the exact amount of water is unknown; deception (gharar) due to uncertainty; and because shurb is not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) since no compensation is due for unauthorized use. The text then gives a practical note: if someone illegally irrigates from another's shurb, there is no financial liability, but the ruler may discipline the offender. This illustrates the Hanafi position that shurb, as a usufruct right attached to land, is not a transferable commodity in ownership contracts but can be transferred through inheritance and bequest of usufruct.

Key Terms
شرب (shurb): Water rights or a channel/stream for irrigation, typically attached to land.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and compensable; shurb is not considered such because unauthorized use does not incur liability.
غرر (gharar): Deception or uncertainty in a contract that can render it invalid.
جهالة (jahl): Ignorance about essential terms, leading to contract invalidity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of shurb (water rights) considered invalid according to this passage?
A) Because it is forbidden by the Quran.
B) Because of ignorance, gharar, and not being valuable property.
C) Because it belongs to the state.
D) Because it is a public resource.

Key Takeaway
Shurb is transferable only through inheritance and bequest of usufruct, not through sale, gift, charity, or bequest of ownership, due to uncertainty and lack of property value.

No Liability for Accidental Water Overflow
States a tort liability rule with ratio legis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for accidental water damage from lawful irrigation and identify the condition of transgression that triggers liability.

Core Idea
Lawful acts causing accidental harm do not create liability without transgression.

In everyday life, water from irrigation may overflow and damage a neighbor's land. Does Islamic law require compensation? The Hanafi school provides a clear principle: if the initial act was lawful and there was no transgression, no liability arises. This lesson unpacks that rule and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The passage describes a man who irrigates his own land lawfully (e.g., for farming).
2
Identify the Harm
Water from his irrigation accidentally flows to another's land and causes damage (flooding or wetness).
3
Apply the Ruling
The text states: no compensation is due because he is not a transgressor (ghayr muta'addi).
4
Understand the Ratio Legis
Liability requires transgression (ta'di). A lawful act without intent to harm or negligence does not trigger daman.
5
Consider Contrasting Views
Other schools may hold that if damage is foreseeable, some precaution is required; but the Hanafi position relies on the lawfulness of the act.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا سقى الرجل ارضه أو مخرها ماء اى ملاها فسال من مائها فى ارض رجل فغرقها اونزت ارض جاره من هذا الماء لم يكن عليه ضمانها لانه غير متعد
ترجمہ اور کسی شخص نے اپنی زمین سیراب کی یا اس کو پانی سے بھر دیا پس اس زمین کا پانی دوسرے شخص کی زمین کی طرف بہا پس اس کو غرق کر دیا یا اس کے پڑوی کی زمین نمناک ہوگئی اس پانی سے تو اس پر اس کا ضمان نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ وہ اس میں تعدی کرنے والا نہیں ہے واللہ اعلم ۔
And if a man irrigates his land or fills it with water, and water from it flows to another man's land and floods it, or his neighbor's land becomes wet from this water, there is no liability upon him because he is not a transgressor.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on tort liability for water damage. If a person lawfully irrigates his own land (or fills it with water for a permissible purpose), and the water accidentally overflows and causes damage to a neighbor's land (e.g., flooding or making it wet), he is not liable for compensation. The reason given is that he is not a transgressor (ghayr muta'addi) because his initial act was lawful. In Hanafi fiqh, liability (daman) arises only from transgression (ta'di) or direct causation with intent. Since the irrigation was permissible and the overflow was unintended, no compensation is due. This is consistent with the principle that one is not liable for consequential damage from a lawful act without negligence.

Key Terms
تعدی (ta'di): Transgression or exceeding lawful bounds; the key condition for liability.
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation for damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, when is a person not liable for water damage to a neighbor's property?
A) When the damage was intentional.
B) When the person's initial act was lawful and there was no transgression.
C) When the damage was minimal.
D) When the neighbor gave permission.

Key Takeaway
Lawful acts causing accidental harm do not create liability; transgression is the key element for daman.

Liability for Water Flooding from Revived Land
Ruling on liability for flooding due to irrigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which liability for water flooding from irrigation becomes obligatory according to the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Liability for flooding only arises when transgression is present—irrigation outside one's turn or beyond one's right.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah explains the Hanafi ruling on liability (daman) when a person irrigates his own land and the water accidentally floods a neighbor's land. The key principle is that no liability exists unless there is transgression (ta'addi). The passage defines transgression in the context of irrigation rights: filling water out of turn or exceeding one's allocated share. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the Hanafi framework for tort liability based on causation and intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
What is the act being considered? It is irrigating one's own land, which is mubah (permissible) in itself.
The act is not inherently harmful.
2
Identify the potential harm
The water may overflow and flood another person's land, causing damage. This is the consequence that may trigger liability.
3
State the general rule
The passage states: no liability (daman) unless there is transgression (ta'addi). The act of irrigation is only a cause (sabab), and for a cause to impose liability, transgression must be present.
Tanbih (note) in the passage emphasizes this reasoning.
4
Define transgression in context
Transgression occurs when the irrigator either fills water outside his designated turn (bari) or fills more than his rightful share (qadr al-haqq). These are the conditions that negate non-liability.
Both conditions must be absent for transgression to be absent.
5
Apply the conditions
If both conditions are met (proper turn and proper amount), no liability. If either condition is violated, transgression exists and liability becomes obligatory. This is the mufta bihi position (upon this is the fatwa).
The fatwa is on the liability case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب احياء الموات
جس نے اپنی زمین میں پانی بھرا تھا اس پر کوئی ضمان نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس کی جانب سے تعدی نہیں پائی گئی اس لئے کہ اس کا مقصد اپنی زمین کو سیراب کرنا تھا نہ کہ دوسرے کی زمین کو بھرنا۔
تنبیہ اس لئے کہ اول کا نعل سبب ہے اور سب پر ضمان واجب ہونے کے لئے تعدی کی شرط ہے اور تعدی ہے نہیں لہذا ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔
لیکن یہ جب ہے جبکہ اس نے اپنی باری میں بھرا ہو اور اپنے حق کے بقدر بھرا ہو اور اگر اپنی باری میں نہیں بھرایا اپنے حق سے زیادہ بھرا بالفاظ دیگر جبکہ اس نے غیر معتاد طریقہ پر بھرا تو یہ تعدی ہے۔ لہذا اب اس کی ضمان واجب ہوگا وعلیہ الفتویٰ۔
مجمع الانهر ص ۵۴۳ ج أسكب الانہر ص ۴۹۷ ج ۲ در مختار ص ۷۸۲ ج ۵
Book of Reviving Dead Land
He who filled water in his own land has no liability upon him because no transgression is found from his side, since his aim was to irrigate his own land, not to fill another's land.
Note: Because the first [cause] is a cause, and liability becoming obligatory on every cause requires the condition of transgression, and there is no transgression, therefore liability will not be obligatory.
But this is when he filled during his own turn and filled to the extent of his right. And if he did not fill during his own turn, or filled more than his right, in other words when he filled in an unusual manner, then this is transgression. Therefore, now his liability will be obligatory, and upon this is the fatwa.
Majma' al-Anhur p. 543 vol. 1, Radd al-Mukhtar p. 497 vol. 2, Durr al-Mukhtar p. 782 vol. 5
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses the ruling on liability (daman) when a person irrigates his own land and the water floods another person's land. The passage type is a ruling with talil (reasoning). The author states the general principle: no liability if there is no transgression (ta'addi). The reasoning is that the person's intention was only to water his own land, not to cause harm to others. The note (tanbih) clarifies that the first cause (the act of filling water) is a cause (sabab), and for liability to arise from a cause, the condition of transgression must be met. Since no transgression is present, no liability. However, the ruling is conditional upon two factors: (1) the person must fill water during his own turn (bari), and (2) he must fill only to the extent of his right (qadr al-haqq). If he fills outside his turn or exceeds his right, that constitutes transgression (ta'addi), and liability becomes obligatory. The fatwa is given on this latter case. This is the Hanafi position as found in the later authoritative texts cited.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for damage caused.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression; exceeding one's right or acting in an unlawful manner.
باری (bari): One's turn or rightful share in irrigation rotation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does liability (daman) become obligatory for flooding caused by irrigating one's own land according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) Whenever the water floods another's land, regardless of intent.
B) Only when the person fills water outside his turn or exceeds his right.
C) Only if the person deliberately harms the neighbour.
D) Never, because the landowner has absolute right to irrigation.

Key Takeaway
Liability for water flooding from irrigation only arises when the irrigator transgresses by filling outside his turn or beyond his right.

The Four Prohibited Drinks in Hanafi Fiqh
Listing and defining four intoxicating drinks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of prohibited drinks according to the Hanafi school and identify the defining characteristic of each.

Core Idea
Four drinks are explicitly haram: grape wine, cooked grape juice (tilā'), date infusion (sakar), and raisin infusion — all if intoxicating.

Al-Quduri begins the chapter on drinks by listing four types that are prohibited. The commentator expands on each, explaining the legal definitions and clarifying how they differ from common assumptions. This lesson will help you recognize exactly what makes each of these drinks haram according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
List the four drinks named: khamr, tilā', sakar, and naqī' al-zabīb. Note that the list is sequential and begins with the most well-known, khamr.
The passage explicitly numbers them as four.
2
Define each type
For each drink, extract the legal definition from the commentary. Khamr is fermented grape juice; tilā' is cooked grape juice with less than 2/3 evaporated; sakar is date infusion; naqī' al-zabīb is raisin infusion when strong and fermented.
The commentary clarifies that 'strong' means capable of causing intoxication.
3
Understand the condition for prohibition
The common element is intoxication. Even if a drink is made from dates or raisins, if it intoxicates, it becomes haram.
This distinguishes the Hanafi from other schools that may limit prohibition to grape wine.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi definition of tilā'
The commentary mentions that tilā' is also called muthallath when exactly two-thirds is gone. This nuance is important for precise application.
Al-Muhit specifies the exact proportion.

Source Text

Book of Drinks. Translation: This book is about the rulings of wines/drinks. Kitab al-Ashriba. Explanation: After issues of drinking, Kitab al-Ashriba was mentioned because there is a verbal similarity between them; both are like branches of the same tree. And because drinking (shurb) is permissible, it was given precedence, while drinks (ashriba) were delayed because some of them are prohibited. The legal meaning of sharab. ... (garbled) ... Translation: This book is named after al-Ashriba, even though ashriba is the plural of sharab, because it contains the rulings of various types of drinks. Explanation: Sharab originally refers to anything drunk: water, syrup, honey, etc. In legal terminology, sharab is that which causes intoxication and renders unconscious. As for the meaning of the author's phrase, a question may arise: why is this place called 'book' when a book is that wherein various types of rulings are mentioned, and ashriba is merely the plural of sharab? Its answer is that the types of ashriba are many and the rulings of all those types are mentioned in it, hence it is appropriate to call it a book. Mulla Sa'di Chali gave a similar answer, and the author of Nata'ij discussed it well. The statement of the four types of wine: al-Quduri said: The prohibited drinks are four: (1) khamr, which is grape juice when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam; (2) cooked juice until less than two-thirds of it is gone – this is the tila' mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir; (3) date infusion which is sakar; (4) raisin infusion when it becomes strong and ferments. Translation: al-Quduri said that the drinks which are prohibited are four: (1) khamr, which is grape juice when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam; (2) grape juice when it is cooked until less than two-thirds of it is gone – this is the tila' mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir; (3) date infusion, which is sakar; (4) raisin infusion when it becomes strong and ferments. Explanation: The author mentioned here four types of drinks, and these four are those which are prohibited. First: raw grape wine when it starts to ferment; 'strong' means that it becomes capable of causing intoxication; this is called khamr. Second type: tila', which is grape juice that has been cooked until slightly less than two-thirds of it is gone. However, in al-Muhit it says that tila' is called 'muthallath' meaning that two-thirds of it are gone and ...
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage introduces Kitab al-Ashriba (The Book of Drinks) from the Hidayah commentary. The commentator first explains why this book follows the chapter on drinking (shurb): there is a verbal relationship between the two words, and the permission of drinking is followed by the prohibition of certain drinks. The legal definition of sharab is given as any intoxicating substance that causes unconsciousness. A discussion follows about the propriety of calling it a 'kitab' (book) since it covers multiple types of drinks. Then the core ruling from al-Quduri is presented: prohibited drinks are four. The first is khamr (unfermented but intoxicating grape juice). The second is grape juice cooked until at least one-third remains (this is tila'). The third is date infusion (sakar). The fourth is raisin infusion (naqi' al-zabib) when it becomes strong and ferments. The commentator clarifies that 'strong' means capable of intoxication. He also notes from al-Muhit that tila' is specifically when two-thirds of the juice is removed (muthallath). This establishes the Hanafi position on intoxicating drinks: the ruling is not limited to grape wine but includes date and raisin infusions.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Grape juice that has fermented, become strong, and thrown foam (intoxicating).
الطلاء (al-ṭilā'): Grape juice cooked until less than two-thirds of it is gone; also called muthallath when exactly two-thirds is gone.
السكر (al-sakar): Date infusion that is intoxicating.
نقيع الزبيب (naqī' al-zabīb): Raisin infusion that becomes strong and ferments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Sakar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school explicitly prohibits four types of intoxicating drinks: grape wine, cooked grape juice (tilā'), date infusion (sakar), and raisin infusion (naqī' al-zabīb).

The Essence of Khamr: Hanafi Definition vs. Opposing View
Defines khamr linguistically and presents the opposing view with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of khamr and identify the three proofs used by the opposing schools to argue that khamr includes all intoxicants.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, khamr is specifically grape wine; other intoxicants are not khamr but are still prohibited.

This passage opens the discussion on intoxicants by first defining what khamr is. The Hanafi school, supported by linguists, defines khamr narrowly as the intoxicating juice of grapes. The opposing view, held by the majority of other schools, argues that khamr is any intoxicating substance. Understanding this dispute is key to knowing which punishment applies and how various intoxicants are legally treated.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi definition of khamr
Identify the two components: the source (grape juice) and the condition (becoming intoxicating). Note that this definition is presented as the well-known position among linguists and scholars.
The Hanafi definition is specific: it must be from grapes and must become intoxicating.
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that 'some people' refers to the three Imams (Shafi‘i, Maliki, Hanbali) and the Zahiris. They assert that khamr is a name for every intoxicant.
This is a broad definition encompassing all substances that intoxicate.
3
Analyze the first proof: Hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr'
The opponents cite this hadith as a direct statement equating all intoxicants with khamr. Consider how the Hanafis might interpret this hadith differently (though the response is not in this passage).
The hadith is accepted by all, but its interpretation is contested.
4
Analyze the second proof: Hadith 'Khamr comes from these two trees'
The opponents argue that mentioning grapes and date palms as sources implies that khamr can come from dates too, thus extending beyond grape wine. They infer that other fruits are also included.
The Hanafis restrict this to those specific fruits, not as a generalization.
5
Analyze the third proof: Etymological derivation from 'mukhamarat al-'aql'
The opponents argue that since the word khamr is derived from 'covering the intellect', any intoxicant that covers the intellect deserves the name. Consider whether this linguistic argument is conclusive.
The Hanafis may argue that etymology does not determine legal definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اما الخمر فالكلام فيها في عشرة مواضع احدها في بيان مائيتها وهى التى من ماء العنب اذا صار مسكرا ولهذا عندنا وهو المعروف عند اهل اللغة واهل الـ
ترجمہ بہر حال خمر پس اس میں دس جگہ میں گفتگو ہے ان میں سے ایک اس کی مائیت کا بیان ہے اور یہ انگور کا کچا پانی ہے جبکہ وہ مسکر ہو جائے اور یہ ہمارے نزدیک ہے اور اہل لغت اور اہل علم کے درمیان یہی معروف ہے۔

ائمہ ثلاثہ اور اصحاب ظاہر کا نقطہ نظر
وقال بعض الناس هو اسم لكل مسكر لقوله عليه السلام كل مسكر خمر و قوله عليه السلام الخمر من هاتين الشجرتين واشار الى الكرمة والنخلة ولانه مشتق من مخامرة العقل وهو موجود في كل مسكر
ترجمہ اور بعض لوگوں نے کہا کہ خمر نام ہے ہر مسکر کا نبی علیہ السلام کے فرمان کی وجہ سے کہ ہر مسکر حرام ہے اور نبی علیہ السلام کے فرمان کی وجہ سے کہ شمران دونوں درختوں سے ہوتی ہے اور اشارہ فرمایا انگور اور کھجور کی جانب اور اس لئے کہ خمر مخامرۃ العقل سے مشتق ہے اور یہ معنی ہر مسکر میں موجود ہیں۔ تشریح ائمہ ثلاثہ اور اصحاب ظاہر کا یہ کہنا ہے کہ ہر مسکر خمر ہے انگور سے بنے یا کسی اور چیز سے اس فریق نے اپنے دعوی پر تین دلیلیں
پیش کی ہیں:
- حدیث کل مسکر خمر
٢- حديث الخمر من هاتين الشجر تين ، کہ ان دونوں درختوں سے جو بنے وہ خمر ہے یعنی انگور اور کھجور سے اس سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ یہ خمر کا اطلاق انگوری شراب کے علاوہ اور کے اوپر بھی ہوتا ہے۔ - خم مشتق ہے مخامرة العقل سے، یعنی عقل کا مستور و مغلوب ہو جانا اور یہ کیفیت ہر شراب سے ہوتی ہے لہذ ا معلوم ہوا کہ ہر
مسکر خمر ہے۔
From the ten discussions: the first discussion is about the essence of khamr. As for khamr, the discussion about it is in ten places. One of them is the explanation of its essence (ma'iyyah), and it is that which is from grape juice when it becomes intoxicating. This is our (Hanafi) position, and it is well-known among linguists and scholars. Translation: In any case, khamr is discussed in ten places. One of them is the explanation of its essence, and it is raw grape juice when it becomes intoxicating. This is our view, and it is well-known among linguists and scholars. Explanation: First, the author will discuss khamr. He says that there are ten discussions about khamr. In the first discussion, he explains its essence. He then defines it: raw grape juice that becomes intoxicating is called khamr; any drink made from other things will not be called khamr. This is well-known among linguists and scholars that khamr refers only to this. The viewpoint of the three Imams and the Zahiris: And some people said that it (khamr) is a name for every intoxicant, because of the Prophet's saying (peace be upon him): 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and his saying: 'Khamr comes from these two trees,' pointing to the grapevine and the date palm, and because it is derived from 'mukhamarat al-'aql' (veiling of the intellect), and this is found in every intoxicant. Translation: And some people said that khamr is the name for every intoxicant, due to the Prophet's saying: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and his saying: 'Khamr comes from these two trees,' pointing to the grapevine and the date palm, and because it is derived from 'mukhamarat al-'aql' (veiling of the intellect), and this meaning is found in every intoxicant. Explanation: The three Imams (Abu Hanifa's opponents: Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) and the Zahiris say that every intoxicant is khamr, whether made from grapes or other things. This party presented three proofs for their claim: (1) The hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr'; (2) The hadith 'Khamr comes from these two trees,' indicating that what is made from grapes and dates is khamr—this also shows that the term khamr applies to other than grape wine; (3) Khamr is derived from 'mukhamarat al-'aql', meaning the covering and overpowering of the intellect, and this quality exists in every intoxicant. Therefore, it is known that every intoxicant is khamr.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the first of ten discussions about khamr in the Book of Intoxicants. The author first states the Hanafi position: khamr is specifically the raw juice of grapes that has become intoxicating. This is also the well-known meaning among linguists and scholars. Then he presents the view of 'some people'—referring to the three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) and the Zahiris—who argue that khamr is a general term for every intoxicant. They provide three proofs: (1) The hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr'; (2) The hadith 'Khamr comes from these two trees,' which mentions grapes and dates; (3) Etymologically, khamr is derived from 'mukhamarat al-'aql' (covering the intellect), which applies to all intoxicants. The text does not yet include the Hanafi response to these arguments, which will follow in subsequent pages. This is a classic intra-Islamic legal dispute about the scope of the term 'khamr' and the implications for prohibition.

Key Terms
مائية الخمر (ma'iyyat al-khamr): The essence or quiddity of khamr; what makes it khamr.
مسكر (muskir): Anything that intoxicates, i.e., causes intoxication.
مخامرة العقل (mukhamarat al-'aql): The veiling or overpowering of the intellect, which is the characteristic effect of intoxicants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on the essence of khamr?
A) Khamr is any intoxicating drink, regardless of source.
B) Khamr is specifically the raw juice of grapes that has become intoxicating.
C) Khamr is only date wine.
D) Khamr is any drink that covers the intellect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines khamr as intoxicating grape juice only, leading to different punishments for non-grape intoxicants compared to the majority view.
